<div id="anp_a" class="d-panel p" style="display: none"><div class="notes p"><section id="historyarticle-meta1" class="history"><p>Received 2022 Mar 1; Accepted 2022 Mar 24; Collection date 2022 Apr.</p></section></div></div>

<div>PMCID: PMC9031868  PMID: <a href="https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/35454986/" class="usa-link">35454986</a>
</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>During space missions, astronauts are faced with a variety of challenges that are unique to spaceflight and that have been known to cause physiological changes in humans over a period of time. Several of these changes occur at the microbiome level, a complex ensemble of microbial communities residing in various anatomic sites of the human body, with a pivotal role in regulating the health and behavior of the host. The microbiome is essential for day-to-day physiological activities, and alterations in microbiome composition and function have been linked to various human diseases. For these reasons, understanding the impact of spaceflight and space conditions on the microbiome of astronauts is important to assess significant health risks that can emerge during long-term missions and to develop countermeasures. Here, we review various conditions that are caused by long-term space exploration and discuss the role of the microbiome in promoting or ameliorating these conditions, as well as space-related factors that impact microbiome composition. The topics explored pertain to microgravity, radiation, immunity, bone health, cognitive function, gender differences and pharmacomicrobiomics. Connections are made between the trifecta of spaceflight, the host and the microbiome, and the significance of these interactions for successful long-term space missions.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> microbiome, spaceflight, space biology, astronaut, human exploration</p></section></section><section id="sec1-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">1. Introduction</h2>
<p>Humans have been exploring space for the last sixty-five years and, with the creation of the International Space Station, have been living and working in space continuously for the past 21 years. Astronauts endure many physiological and psychological changes while in space because of altered gravity, radiation, and confinement, to name but a few factors. While some spaceflight side effects are well known, such as bone loss [<a href="#B1-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-life-12-00495">1</a>], muscle atrophy [<a href="#B2-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-life-12-00495">2</a>], altered ocular structure [<a href="#B3-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-life-12-00495">3</a>], cognitive decline [<a href="#B4-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-life-12-00495">4</a>], fluid redistribution [<a href="#B5-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-life-12-00495">5</a>] and immune dysregulation [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>], others, such as the taxonomic and functional changes of the astronaut microbiome, have been less studied, with the impact on astronaut health even less so. Understanding how the human microbiome adapts to space travel and how this influences astronaut health, pre-, post- and in-flight, is essential in reaching our goal of long-duration human exploration in low Earth orbit (LEO) and beyond. This review will discuss changes in the astronaut microbiome as a result of spaceflight (and other stressors pre- and post-flight), how these changes may impact astronaut health, and the resulting consequences for long-duration spaceflight. We also provide recommendations for industry and government entities designing future manned missions to the Moon and Mars on how to incorporate microbiome data into their planning and ways in which the microbiome can be targeted or manipulated to ensure successful long-duration human exploration beyond low Earth orbit.</p></section><section id="sec2-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">2. Human Microbiome</h2>
<p>Our body consists of trillions of bacteria that are on par with the number of human cells we have [<a href="#B7-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7-life-12-00495">7</a>]. The gastrointestinal tract (GIT) alone harbors 100 trillion bacteria, consisting of 1000 different species, 7000 strains and 3.3 million non-redundant microbial genes [<a href="#B8-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-life-12-00495">8</a>,<a href="#B9-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-life-12-00495">9</a>,<a href="#B10-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-life-12-00495">10</a>]. The skin bacterial communities on a typical hand consist of &gt;150 species, with only 13% similarity amongst different individuals [<a href="#B11-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-life-12-00495">11</a>]. Other sites have simpler bacterial communities, like the healthy vagina, which is mainly composed of <em>Lactobacillus</em> [<a href="#B12-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12-life-12-00495">12</a>]. Human breast tissue, once thought of as sterile, is now accepted as having a stable microbial population [<a href="#B13-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-life-12-00495">13</a>,<a href="#B14-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-life-12-00495">14</a>]. These microbial communities, found in and outside our body, are referred to as the “human microbiome”, a term first coined in 2001 by Joshua Lederberg to refer to the “ecological community of commensal, symbiotic and pathogenic microorganisms that literally share our body space” [<a href="#B15-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-life-12-00495">15</a>]. These microorganisms consist of archaea, eukaryotes (fungi and protists) and viruses, with bacteria making up the majority of organisms present (99%) [<a href="#B10-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-life-12-00495">10</a>]. While the terms “human microbiome” and “human microbiota” are often used interchangeably, the latter refers to the microbial taxa associated with humans, while the former refers to the collection of microbial taxa and their genes [<a href="#B16-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-life-12-00495">16</a>].</p>
<p>The microbial communities that colonize various parts of our body are important in promoting health, by synthesizing vitamins the host cannot make, salvaging energy from indigestible compounds, creating a competitive environment to prevent pathogen colonization, promoting maturation and regulation of the immune system, contributing to vascular development and angiogenesis or enhancing the integrity of the epithelial barrier [<a href="#B17-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-life-12-00495">17</a>,<a href="#B18-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-life-12-00495">18</a>,<a href="#B19-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-life-12-00495">19</a>,<a href="#B20-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-life-12-00495">20</a>,<a href="#B21-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-life-12-00495">21</a>,<a href="#B22-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-life-12-00495">22</a>,<a href="#B23-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-life-12-00495">23</a>]. These benefits are achieved by a delicate balance of commensals, symbionts and pathobionts that collectively make up one’s microbiome. If this balance is disrupted even slightly, a breakdown in homeostasis will occur, leading to disease [<a href="#B24-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-life-12-00495">24</a>]. Microbial differences have been documented between healthy and diseased individuals with periodontitis [<a href="#B25-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-life-12-00495">25</a>], inflammatory bowel disease [<a href="#B26-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-life-12-00495">26</a>], psoriasis [<a href="#B27-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-life-12-00495">27</a>], asthma [<a href="#B28-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-life-12-00495">28</a>], bacterial vaginosis [<a href="#B29-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-life-12-00495">29</a>], colorectal cancer [<a href="#B30-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-life-12-00495">30</a>] and breast cancer [<a href="#B31-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-life-12-00495">31</a>]. These observed microbial differences are not simply a consequence of the diseased state creating an environment that selects for certain bacteria, as studies have shown that healthy animals transplanted with feces from those with obesity [<a href="#B32-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-life-12-00495">32</a>], colitis [<a href="#B33-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-life-12-00495">33</a>] and colorectal cancer [<a href="#B34-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-life-12-00495">34</a>] then go on to develop disease. Shifts in bacterial profiles not only have consequences at the site of origin but can have distal site effects as well. For example, alterations in the gut microbiota can have effects on the brain [<a href="#B35-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-life-12-00495">35</a>,<a href="#B36-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-life-12-00495">36</a>], liver [<a href="#B37-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-life-12-00495">37</a>,<a href="#B38-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-life-12-00495">38</a>], and pancreas [<a href="#B39-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39-life-12-00495">39</a>], while microbial shifts in the oral cavity can be detrimental to cardiovascular health [<a href="#B40-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-life-12-00495">40</a>].</p></section><section id="sec3-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">3. Spaceflight and the Astronaut Microbiome</h2>
<section id="sec3dot1-life-12-00495"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1. Spaceflight Hazards: Conditions and Challenges Facing Space Travelers</h3>
<p>Long-term spaceflight and especially deep-space exploratory missions represent an extreme environment for humans that demands adaptations to both physical and psychological stressors (<a href="#life-12-00495-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>) [<a href="#B41-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41-life-12-00495">41</a>]. As such, a large part of spaceflight research focuses on the effects of space exposure on humans, with the aim to elucidate physiological, psychological and behavioral health risks and the necessary solutions to combat them [<a href="#B42-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-life-12-00495">42</a>]. Much of the existing knowledge about spaceflight hazards derives from crewed missions in LEO, such as those onboard the International Space Station (ISS), and analog missions on Earth. Additionally, a significant amount of data have been generated by experiments carried out in ground-based facilities, simulating different space conditions. This information, however, may provide inaccurate estimates into the risks connected to deep-space missions (outside LEO), which involve more time spent in space during a single mission than that hitherto experienced by astronauts (i.e., on average 7 months). Moreover, deep-space exploration will venture beyond the protective effects of Earth’s geomagnetic field; therefore, crewmembers on upcoming missions to the Moon, Mars and beyond will be exposed to significantly higher doses of cosmic radiation. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-12-00495-f001"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=9031868_life-12-00495-g001.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1e95/9031868/483a28252efd/life-12-00495-g001.jpg" loading="lazy" height="889" width="718" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-12-00495-f001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>Biological features of spaceflight.</strong> In space, microgravity, radiation, and confinement in a closed environment thousands of miles away from Earth pose health risks and drive many physiological changes and psychological effects seen during spaceflight. Figure created with BioRender.com (accessed on 18 February 2022).</p></figcaption></figure><section id="sec3dot1dot1-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1.1. Radiation</h4>
<p>The global radiation dose for astronauts is affected by galactic cosmic rays (GCRs) originating from outside the solar system, which include high atomic number and energy (HZE) ions and high-intensity solar particle events (SPEs) that produce pulses of heavy ion and energetic proton radiation [<a href="#B43-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-life-12-00495">43</a>]. It is estimated that a one-year stay on the lunar surface would result in absorbed doses for crews in the range from 100 to 120 mGy (milligray), whereas during a three-year Mars mission, transit and stay included, the values would increase to 450 mGy [<a href="#B44-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-life-12-00495">44</a>]. In comparison, the predicted doses for 6- to 12-month ISS missions range instead from roughly 30 to 120 mGy [<a href="#B44-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-life-12-00495">44</a>]. For reference, 4.5 Gy has been indicated as the LD<sub>50</sub> value for human cells (median lethal dose) [<a href="#B45-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-life-12-00495">45</a>]. During SPEs, astronauts may additionally be exposed to an extremely high dose of protons (i.e., up to 1 Gy or more) that could lead to acute radiation sickness (ARS) [<a href="#B46-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-life-12-00495">46</a>,<a href="#B47-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-life-12-00495">47</a>]. Although radiation derived from SPEs is effectively absorbed by the shielding material of a spacecraft or well-designed spacesuit, not all SPEs are predictable and can therefore pose a real threat to astronauts, especially during planetary extravehicular activity (EVA), which would take place more frequently than during a 6-month ISS stay [<a href="#B48-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B48-life-12-00495">48</a>]. </p>
<p>The consequences of chronic exposure to radiation, and to CGR in particular, is concerning, due to the high energy, high penetrability and ionizing nature of their most hazardous components, the HZE particles [<a href="#B49-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B49-life-12-00495">49</a>]. These particles are so penetrating that shielding can only partially protect against exposure and the large emission of secondary neutrons that may follow, posing an additional hazard to the crew [<a href="#B50-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50-life-12-00495">50</a>]. The types of radiation encountered in space are different from terrestrial radiation, such as X-rays and gamma rays, and induce distinct patterns of DNA double-strand breaks and disease outcomes, resulting in epigenetic changes and persistently high levels of oxidative damage and tissue inflammation following exposure [<a href="#B51-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-life-12-00495">51</a>]. This is relevant because of the association of oxidative stress with the etiology of several human diseases, including cancer, cardiovascular and neurodegenerative disorders (e.g., altered sensory perception, neurovestibular problems, etc. [<a href="#B52-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-life-12-00495">52</a>]), gastrointestinal diseases [<a href="#B53-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B53-life-12-00495">53</a>], and the possible correlation between spaceflight and the onset of a number of diseases normally related to aging [<a href="#B54-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-life-12-00495">54</a>]. Exposure to space radiation, especially when considering that heavy ions are more effective per unit dose in causing solid cancer compared to gamma-rays [<a href="#B55-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-life-12-00495">55</a>], can therefore have a detrimental impact on the quality of life during and post flight. Visual disturbances appear to also be correlated to radiation exposure, as indicated by reports from past Apollo, Skylab and MIR missions where astronauts experienced flashes of light moving across their visual field, possibly due to alteration in perception caused by ionizing radiation [<a href="#B56-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B56-life-12-00495">56</a>]. An additional aspect to consider when assessing radiation-related risks are the non-targeted effects (NTEs) of radiation, which increase biological effectiveness for low doses of high linear energy transfer radiation (LET), such as HZE particles, and may lead to alterations in cell signaling and/or genomic instability of cell progeny [<a href="#B55-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-life-12-00495">55</a>,<a href="#B57-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57-life-12-00495">57</a>]. </p></section><section id="sec3dot1dot2-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1.2. Microgravity</h4>
<p>Along with radiation, microgravity—a condition in which the gravity level is almost zero but not neutralized—represents another major health hazard related to spaceflight. While astronauts mostly experience microgravity during spaceflight and onboard the ISS, they do experience intermediate periods of hypergravity (e.g., 3–6 G) during launch and ascent and upon descent back through the Earth’s atmosphere [<a href="#B58-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-life-12-00495">58</a>]. Additionally, various levels of altered gravity are found on other planetary bodies, such as on the Earth’s Moon—one-sixth gravity (G)—and Mars—one-third gravity (G)—(hypogravity) [<a href="#B42-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-life-12-00495">42</a>]. </p>
<p>Spaceflight and ground-based analog experiments have shown that altered gravity, as well as the transition through various levels of gravity, subject biological systems (i.e., humans, animals, plants) to varying levels of stress with negative consequences. Specifically, microgravity can induce cellular and molecular alterations with changes to the genome, epigenome, and proteome, connected with a range of pathologies [<a href="#B42-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-life-12-00495">42</a>]. In humans, exposure to microgravity can influence several body systems such as the neurovestibular, cardiovascular, musculoskeletal, bone metabolic and immune-hematological system [<a href="#B59-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-life-12-00495">59</a>,<a href="#B60-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B60-life-12-00495">60</a>]. For example, altered body fluid distribution occurs during microgravity and this headward shift of fluids, including blood, leads to a compensatory cardiovascular system change, with increased intracranial pressure, cerebrospinal pressure or inner ear fluid pressure, decreased leg volume, puffiness in the face and even long-term ocular damage [<a href="#B61-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B61-life-12-00495">61</a>]. According to the “fluid shift” theory, this increased pressure is the root of “space motion sickness”, a set of symptoms that impair operational performance of 60–80% of astronauts. Additional interconnected abnormalities include morphological changes in the white and grey matter of the brain following long-duration spaceflight (average of 171 days), which have been attributed to the structural neuroplasticity of the sensorimotor system, in an effort to adapt motor strategies to maintain physiological homeostasis and ensure proper behavioral output in space [<a href="#B3-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-life-12-00495">3</a>]. Lack of gravitational loading also results in muscle atrophy, especially in the lower extremities, accompanied by functional and structural alterations. While muscular loss has been linked to reduced muscular activity and hypokinesia due to limited movement inside the spacecraft, structural changes seem to be related to both a higher level of muscle protein degradation enzymes and a decrease in protein synthesis [<a href="#B59-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-life-12-00495">59</a>]. Extended exposure to microgravity also results in bone loss due to insufficient bone formation compared with bone resorption, and this reduction in bone mass and strength during spaceflight increases the risk of bone fracture, even upon return to Earth [<a href="#B62-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62-life-12-00495">62</a>]. As reported for radiation, similarities have been observed between the adaptive response to microgravity in humans and aging, since both prompt the decline of almost every body system [<a href="#B5-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-life-12-00495">5</a>]. Though several phenotypic changes resemble those determined by aging under standard gravitational conditions, the magnitude and speed of some processes (bone loss, among others) is far greater (i.e., 1% loss/month) than that which is typically seen during aging. Hence, this is of critical concern when considering the effects of long-duration spaceflight [<a href="#B1-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-life-12-00495">1</a>]. </p></section><section id="sec3dot1dot3-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1.3. Psychological Stressors</h4>
<p>In addition to the aforementioned stressors, several psychological and social issues have been demonstrated to affect the crew during extended separation from society in a closed and confined environment. They may include homesickness and loneliness, apathy, interpersonal stressors, and sexual attraction/tension [<a href="#B63-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B63-life-12-00495">63</a>]. A constricted living environment can lead to isolation, loss of spatial capacity, increased anxiety and depression, which can be accompanied by symptoms such as hallucinations, reduced consciousness and poor bodily coordination [<a href="#B64-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-12-00495">64</a>,<a href="#B65-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65-life-12-00495">65</a>]. Additional psychological triggers range from external physical hazards such as space debris and vehicular malfunction to gravitational shifts and outer space radiation [<a href="#B65-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65-life-12-00495">65</a>]. The ability of ionizing radiation to modulate the psycho-emotional status and, specifically, to exert an anxiogenic effect on the central nervous system was shown in rats exposed to doses related to deep-space missions [<a href="#B66-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-life-12-00495">66</a>]. In addition, diet has a critical role in both the physiological and the psychological health of space travelers. The spacecraft environment, in particular the lack of ultraviolet exposure, increased carbon dioxide levels, the spacesuit atmosphere, etc., can affect nutrition requirements for long-duration missions. Crewmembers may even experience a reduction in their food intake, dubbed “anorexia in space” which may be linked to microgravity, alterations in the circadian rhythm (continuous light environment of space missions) and “menu fatigue”, which not only affects appetite but the proper functioning of the gastrointestinal system [<a href="#B64-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-12-00495">64</a>]. Hence, nutritious and palatable food is necessary not only to meet nutritional requirements and avoid deficiency diseases, but also to keep astronauts psychologically healthy [<a href="#B67-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-life-12-00495">67</a>]. </p>
<p>Excessive exposure to noise, mainly due to equipment and crew activities, may represent another stressor compromising well-being as well as sleeping patterns [<a href="#B68-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B68-life-12-00495">68</a>]. As a matter of fact, the duration of an astronaut’s sleep is reportedly reduced to around 6 to 6.5 h/day during missions [<a href="#B69-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-life-12-00495">69</a>]. Psychosomatic disorders (e.g., headaches, fear of illness, gastroenteric problems), consisting of distressing physical symptoms which are not fully explained by a real physical condition, have also been reported from space [<a href="#B64-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-12-00495">64</a>]. Similarly, post-return personality changes and psychiatric problems have been observed in space travelers [<a href="#B70-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B70-life-12-00495">70</a>]. Other stress factors that may arise in a multicultural crew are related to language barriers, stereotypes and cultural misunderstandings [<a href="#B67-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-life-12-00495">67</a>]. Additionally, new psychological stressors could appear in deep-space missions that were not present in missions closer to Earth, and already-known issues could be intensified, leading to stress and problematic behaviors that may interfere with a crew’s productivity and relationships [<a href="#B71-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71-life-12-00495">71</a>]. For example, during a multi-year spaceflight such as that required for a mission to Mars, the crew would be confronted with no access to most of the mitigation strategies currently in place on the ISS, such as real-time communications with family and ground-based mission control and the view of Earth, that contribute to their psychological well-being [<a href="#B48-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B48-life-12-00495">48</a>]. Based on the “Earth-out-of-view phenomenon”, humans traveling in outer space might start feeling unconnected to Earth and to family and friends [<a href="#B72-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72-life-12-00495">72</a>]. On the other hand, delays in crew–ground communication (up to 22 min on Mars) would require astronauts to operate more autonomously from mission control [<a href="#B73-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B73-life-12-00495">73</a>]. Furthermore, people on a Mars expedition will need to depend on local resources to generate water and fuel for the return home and thus, the psychology of this dependance is an important issue to be considered [<a href="#B70-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B70-life-12-00495">70</a>]. In this context, a greater likelihood of withdrawal, territorial behavior and asthenia may occur [<a href="#B74-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B74-life-12-00495">74</a>]. The latter is a problematic syndrome quite commonly observed during long-duration missions that produces fatigue, irritability, attention and concentration difficulties, along with heightened perceptual sensitivities, physical weakness, sleep and appetite problems, etc. [<a href="#B64-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-life-12-00495">64</a>,<a href="#B75-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B75-life-12-00495">75</a>].</p></section><section id="sec3dot1dot4-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1.4. Additional Risk Factors </h4>
<p>Psychological stress, circadian rhythms, and sleep are key factors strongly connected with one another, as well as with the immune system. This is especially relevant since microbial infection is another challenge facing space travelers. The isolation of opportunistic and pathogenic microorganisms from spacecraft and space stations has been frequently reported [<a href="#B76-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B76-life-12-00495">76</a>,<a href="#B77-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77-life-12-00495">77</a>,<a href="#B78-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B78-life-12-00495">78</a>] and several studies have demonstrated that spaceflight affects both the immune system (i.e., immune dysregulation) [<a href="#B79-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-life-12-00495">79</a>] and microbial physiology (i.e., enhanced virulence, biofilm formation and antibiotics resistance) [<a href="#B80-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-life-12-00495">80</a>], leading to increased risk of disease [<a href="#B81-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-life-12-00495">81</a>]. Additionally, the proximity of crewmembers to one another in the spacecraft can promote the spread of secondary infections [<a href="#B82-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B82-life-12-00495">82</a>]. Due to limited access to medical care during a mission, any possibility of infection should be prevented to ensure the health and safety of the astronauts and to maximize the success rate of the space mission [<a href="#B83-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B83-life-12-00495">83</a>].</p>
<p>Crews do not experience the aforementioned stressors independently; therefore, it is important to consider the combined effects of these space environment threats on human physiology, psychology, and performance. These stressful conditions, collectively referred to as “the space exposome” [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>], may also exacerbate complex health problems in astronauts embarking on long-duration missions [<a href="#B3-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-life-12-00495">3</a>]. Space agencies have adopted a number of operational mitigations, direct and indirect countermeasures, whose aim is to lessen the clinical risks related to the physical and psychological stressors associated with space flight. These encompass strict exercise regimes, the use of devices to induce footward fluid shift (i.e., lower body negative pressure, LBNP) [<a href="#B2-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-life-12-00495">2</a>], pre-flight quarantine to reduce contact with potential pathogens [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>], radiation-shielding spacesuits [<a href="#B84-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B84-life-12-00495">84</a>], the use of HEPA air filters and in-line water filters in the spacecraft, nutritional supplementation, vaccination, psychological support, etc. [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>]. </p>
<p>While some of the changes induced by exposure to space conditions (e.g., reduction in some motor functions) reportedly disappear shortly or a few months after the end of LEO missions [<a href="#B85-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B85-life-12-00495">85</a>], it should be noted that risk assessments and mitigation for lunar visit/habitation, deep-space journey/habitation and planetary missions remain uncertain [<a href="#B86-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B86-life-12-00495">86</a>]. If certain medical risks, such as nutrition, infection, psychological impact and even death, appear to be common to all mission profiles, other risks are actually unique to individual scenarios [<a href="#B87-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B87-life-12-00495">87</a>]. For instance, risks associated with lunar missions—e.g., lunar surface operations, a lunar outpost, etc.—along with radiation, microgravity and the aforementioned psychological issues, also include exposure to hazardous materials such as rocket fuel, lunar dust (regolith), micrometeorite impact damage, and extremes of temperature [<a href="#B87-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B87-life-12-00495">87</a>]. Similarly, perchlorates in the Martian dust would be a concern in terms of contamination of the habitats and of inhalation of harmful particles, posing a great risk to the lung already affected by altered pulmonary deposition induced by microgravity [<a href="#B88-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B88-life-12-00495">88</a>]. The optimization of current and novel countermeasures will therefore be critical. </p></section></section><section id="sec3dot2-life-12-00495"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.2. Effects of Spaceflight Conditions on the Human Microbiome</h3>
<p>It is apparent that the space environment imposes several challenges to human physiology. Specific space environment factors, such as microgravity and radiation, are thought to also induce changes in microbiome composition (i.e., dysbiosis) [<a href="#B89-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-life-12-00495">89</a>], which may alter host–microbe interactions and adversely affect immune function and metabolism, thereby representing a risk to astronaut health, especially during long-term spaceflight missions. </p>
<p>A list of reviewed microbiome studies pertaining to spaceflight as well as ground-based simulations (conducted through to 2021) is provided in <a href="#life-12-00495-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>.</p>
<section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="life-12-00495-t001"><h4 class="obj_head">Table 1.</h4>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/life-12-00495-t001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section><section id="sec3dot2dot1-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.2.1. Gut Microbiome</h4>
<p>The gut microbiome, often described as the “virtual organ of the human body” [<a href="#B104-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B104-life-12-00495">104</a>], will play a crucial and significant role in maintaining astronaut health during space travel, as it does for humans on Earth. High microbiome diversity and richness are generally considered a hallmark of a healthy gut ecosystem; however, there is still no consensus on the actual health-related values [<a href="#B105-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B105-life-12-00495">105</a>]. Healthy adult humans characteristically harbor more than 1000 species of bacteria, with <em>Bacteroidetes</em> and <em>Firmicutes</em> being the dominant phyla [<a href="#B106-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B106-life-12-00495">106</a>]. While <em>Bacteroidetes</em> (recently renamed as <em>Bacteroidota</em> [<a href="#B107-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B107-life-12-00495">107</a>]) are connected with immunomodulation and augmented immune reactions through synthesis of cytokines, <em>Firmicutes</em> are involved in the metabolism, nutrition, and regulation of hunger and satiety, via short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) synthesis [<a href="#B108-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B108-life-12-00495">108</a>]. Exposure to various stressors can change the stability of the gut microbiota, impacting its composition and functions, and increasing the relative abundance of potentially harmful bacteria (e.g., opportunistic pathogens) [<a href="#B109-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B109-life-12-00495">109</a>,<a href="#B110-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B110-life-12-00495">110</a>,<a href="#B111-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B111-life-12-00495">111</a>,<a href="#B112-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B112-life-12-00495">112</a>,<a href="#B113-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B113-life-12-00495">113</a>]. </p>
<p>Studies have been conducted to monitor changes in the gut microbiome under real or simulated spaceflight conditions, involving both animal and human subjects. Culture-dependent techniques, substantially used in the past, have been in recent years replaced by high-throughput omics technologies—i.e., 16S ribosomal RNA gene analysis and metagenomic sequencing—which by detecting and measuring also non-cultivable strains have allowed a more comprehensive characterization of the microbiome structure and its biological functions [<a href="#B114-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B114-life-12-00495">114</a>]. Despite the advent of new technologies, the number of reports is still limited, and the dynamics of the gut microbiome during space missions are yet to be fully elucidated. </p>
<p>A recent analysis of fecal samples from mice flown on the ISS for 37 days revealed spaceflight-associated changes in the gut microbiome as compared to the ground controls. These changes consisted of an altered community structure (i.e., an elevated <em>Firmicutes</em>-to-<em>Bacteroides</em> ratio), higher abundance of bacteria belonging to the order <em>Clostridiales</em> and a reduction in the number of <em>Lactobacillales</em> (organisms usually considered probiotics), with these changes connected to an altered liver transcriptome [<a href="#B89-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-life-12-00495">89</a>]. The richness of the microbiome, however, remained unchanged. Similar trends were previously observed in a study reporting the effects of 13-day spaceflight on female mice and were confirmed using a ground-based model of microgravity [<a href="#B92-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B92-life-12-00495">92</a>]. The above results are comparable with data collected from Voorhies et al. (2019), which assessed the impact of long-term space travel on the crew microbiome and surrounding ISS environment, and the consequence on human health [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. A total of nine crewmembers were sampled pre-, during and post-flight, comparing 6-month and 1-year missions. Results indicated that the microbiome composition became more similar between astronauts over the course of the mission, mostly due to a drop in the abundance of a few bacterial taxa [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. It was revealed that 13 of 17 genera, whose abundance significantly changed in space, were <em>Firmicutes</em>, mainly belonging to the order <em>Clostridiales</em>. Specifically, the authors reported higher proportions of <em>Faecalibacterium</em>, which is known to be a beneficial SCFA producer (i.e., butyrate), but also of genera associated with chronic intestinal inflammation, such as <em>Parasutterella</em>. At the same time, it was observed a greater than five-fold reduction in the relative abundance of <em>Akkermansia</em>, a genus with anti-inflammatory properties, which according to the authors, could play a role in the moderate increase in the inflammatory immune response observed in the crew during spaceflight. Accordingly, the administration in space of prebiotics or next-generation probiotics, such as <em>Akkermansia</em>, has been proposed [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. Previous sampling campaigns carried out for the Skylab program had also reported a decrease in the diversity of the gastrointestinal community, although the overall microbial count went up following space flight. The data showed an increase in certain pathogenic strains, i.e., <em>Serratia marcescens</em> and <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em>, the latter of which was found to be transmitted among astronauts, thereby indicating the transmission of pathogens between individuals in the spaceship environment [<a href="#B91-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B91-life-12-00495">91</a>]. </p>
<p>The “Twins Study” has provided the unique chance to evaluate the impact of long-duration flight on the gut microbiome by comparing profiles of an astronaut and his twin, who, by remaining on Earth, served as ground control. The study aimed to monitor various health parameters including changes in ocular, cardiovascular, cognitive and immune functions, as well as cell-specific changes in physiology, transcriptome, proteome, metabolome, epigenome, and telomere length, while controlling for genetics [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>]. Although each subject maintained individual microbiome characteristics, more changes were found to occur in the microbial community composition and function during the flight period. In line with what previously observed in other spaceflight experiments [<a href="#B89-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-life-12-00495">89</a>], a specific increase in the <em>Firmicutes</em>-to-<em>Bacteroidetes</em> ratio was detected during the 1-year flight period onboard the ISS. Interestingly, this was a transient change, not persisting upon return to Earth, indicating a rebound across the microbial ecosystem of the gastrointestinal tract. Moreover, as anticipated above, microbiome richness composition remained substantially unchanged. </p>
<p>Alterations in the composition and functionality of the gut microbiome can be induced even by short-term space travel. Liu et al. (2020) reported shifts between dominant genera in the microbiome during space missions of 15 and 35 days that led to increased abundance of <em>Bacteroides</em>. By contrast, the probiotic taxa <em>Lactobacillus</em> and <em>Bifidobacterium</em> appeared reduced, possibly affecting host immune function [<a href="#B81-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-life-12-00495">81</a>]. Individual specificity was, however, uncompromised. These changes were accompanied by fluctuations in virulence and antibiotic resistance genes and in mobile genetic elements, and by an increase in genes related to biofilm formation [<a href="#B81-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-life-12-00495">81</a>], which are suggestive of enhanced virulence potential and possibility of infection by opportunistic pathogens or pathobiont of the gut microbiota in space missions [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. Changes in the intestinal microbiota were also reported in response to increased gravity, a condition experienced by astronauts during specific flight phases such as launch, ascent and descent [<a href="#B58-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-life-12-00495">58</a>]. A study conducted on mice by Alauzet et al. (2019) revealed disruption of intracaecal microbiota following exposure to hypergravity (3G) for 21 days, which resulted in a decrease in the <em>Firmicutes</em>-to-<em>Bacteroidetes</em> ratio, however without alteration of mucosal integrity [<a href="#B96-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96-life-12-00495">96</a>]. Of interest, a significant diminution of <em>Proteobacteria</em> was observed at 3G, while the opposite was observed for potentially deleterious taxa, such as members of the <em>Paraprevotella</em> genus which have been described as being more prevalent in intestinal lumen of patients with colorectal cancer [<a href="#B116-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B116-life-12-00495">116</a>]. </p>
<p>Rearrangements in microbiome composition have also been observed in ground-based analog missions. With the MARS500 study, the temporal dynamics of the gut microbiome of six male crewmembers were monitored over 520 days of isolation within an analog Mars-surface habitat [<a href="#B94-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94-life-12-00495">94</a>]. During the stay in the spacecraft-like habitat, the crewmembers performed realistic activities of a round-trip mission to Mars, including operative work, scientific experiments, exercise and even simulated emergency events, and their access to water and food, whose composition reflected the diet used in the ISS, was limited as in a real space flight [<a href="#B94-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94-life-12-00495">94</a>]. Fecal samples were collected not only during the mission but 10 days before and up until 6 months following the return to normal life, making it the longest controlled human confinement study conducted to date. In the first stage of the mission, an increase in the <em>Bacteroides</em>-to-<em>Firmicutes</em> ratio was detected, which is consistent with observations from another analog mission, the “Skylab Medical Experiments Altitude Test” carried out in the 1970s in a 56-day confinement environment [<a href="#B117-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B117-life-12-00495">117</a>]. Additionally, the study revealed decreased proportions of some SCFA producers, especially <em>Faecalibacterium prausnitzii</em> (a butyrate producer), that reached their lowest value at about 1 year of confinement. Not only were increased relative abundance of <em>Bacteroides</em> and a decrease in SCFA producers observed in all subjects involved in the study, but these findings paralleled psychological and physiological data that hint at the presence of both mental and physical stress. The authors thereby suggested that changes in this kind in gut microbiota components could be used for the early diagnosis of potential health warnings. MARS500 project data from early (days 7–45) and late (days 420–520) fecal samples were recently reanalyzed using improved 16S rRNA gene amplicon bioinformatics technology [<a href="#B102-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B102-life-12-00495">102</a>]. The reanalysis confirmed a significant alteration in relative abundance of the microbiome throughout the period of the study, which included species known to influence inflammation and glucose homeostasis in their host (e.g., <em>F. prausnitzii</em>, <em>Ruminococcus bromii</em>, <em>Blautia luti</em>, <em>Anaerostipes hadrus</em>, <em>Roseburia faecis</em>, and <em>Lactobacillus rogosae</em>) and was consistent with crewmembers’ symptoms. Moreover, a certain level of species overlap could be observed between crewmembers and their habitat: 49 species were shared, representing 49% and 12% of the human and environmental microbiome diversity, respectively. </p>
<p>Convergence in the microbiota composition of crewmembers (<em>n</em> = 3) was also observed in a study monitoring a 105-day analog mission that took place in the Chinese Lunar Palace 1 (LP1) [<a href="#B95-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B95-life-12-00495">95</a>]. Results also showed the beneficial influence of the LP1 bioregenerative life-support system (a closed ecosystem integrating efficient higher plant cultivation) dietary structure and a balanced lifestyle (daily diet, living and working activities strictly followed a regular schedule) on the maintenance of a healthy gut microbiota. A high-plant and high-fiber diet resulted in higher microbiome diversity and richness and specifically, a higher abundance of bacteria of the genera <em>Lachnospira, Faecalibacterium</em> and <em>Blautia</em> of the <em>Firmicutes</em> phylum that are known to metabolize dietary polysaccharides and to have anti-inflammatory properties [<a href="#B118-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B118-life-12-00495">118</a>]. </p>
<p>These studies are indicative that modifications in the astronaut gut microbiome occur during spaceflight and analog missions, but the full implications of these findings are yet to be determined in relation to the risks for human health and performance during space travel [<a href="#B119-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B119-life-12-00495">119</a>]. Analyses showed that changes to gut microbiome composition are reversible, with at least partial reversal occurring in the order of days to weeks following return to Earth or completion of an analog mission [<a href="#B89-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-life-12-00495">89</a>,<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>,<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. However, with extended exploration missions, microbial changes will persist for longer as a consequence of flight duration, due to the limited opportunity of microbial replenishment as compared to individuals on Earth, and this may have long-lasting and serious side-effects, even upon return to Earth [<a href="#B77-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77-life-12-00495">77</a>]. It is also unknown how long after extended spaceflight the microbiome will return to pre-flight levels. </p>
<p>The combination of space stressors can also impact the gut metabolome, as observed by Casero et al. (2017), where continuous exposure to space-type radiation led to functional shifts in metabolic pathways dominated by microbiome-specific enzymatic reactions [<a href="#B93-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-life-12-00495">93</a>]. Changes in small-molecule markers of microbial metabolism were also observed in the “Twins Study”, along with particularly low levels of metabolites with anti-inflammatory activity [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>]. In this context, looking into the metabolome of the gut microbiota and its modulation as a result of the spaceflight environment may be promising to aid the development of countermeasures that include the use of prebiotics, probiotics and postbiotics to prevent and mitigate pathological effects in astronauts [<a href="#B112-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B112-life-12-00495">112</a>].</p></section><section id="sec3dot2dot2-life-12-00495"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">3.2.2. Skin, Oropharyngeal and Nasal Microbiome</h4>
<p>Microbiome communities residing in the nose and oral cavities and on the skin have also been investigated in relation to spaceflight-induced compositional and functional changes. Located at the entrance of the upper respiratory tract, the nasal cavity and oropharynx serve as the physical barrier to the invasion of pathogens as well as habitats for a large number of commensals and opportunistic pathogens that live in the host as part of the normal microfloa [<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>]. In the skin, most resident microbes behave as commensal or mutualistic under steady-state conditions and play important roles in the maturation and homeostasis of cutaneous immunity [<a href="#B120-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B120-life-12-00495">120</a>]. The disruption of the balance of the microbiota associated with the human respiratory tract and to the skin may result in an increased susceptibility to infection and to the overgrowth of pathogens (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>). On that basis, surveillance of the microbiota structure may be crucial to counteract significant health risks during long-term flight and to guide medical treatment. Moreover, since astronaut skin is the primary source of spacecraft surface contamination, monitoring skin alterations and alterations in the skin-associated microbiome is key to managing astronaut health as well as in the maintenance of space stations, spaceships and spacecraft equipment [<a href="#B121-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B121-life-12-00495">121</a>]. </p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="life-12-00495-f002"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 2.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=9031868_life-12-00495-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/1e95/9031868/67d67242c1f3/life-12-00495-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="507" width="729" alt="Figure 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/life-12-00495-f002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p><strong>Impact of spaceflight-induced microbiome alterations on human biology and physiology.</strong> Physiology on Earth (in blue) is compared to physiology in space (in red). Factors that can influence the microbiome—i.e., diet—or that can both influence and be influenced by the microbiome—i.e., immunity—are also shown. Central illustration (human body) “I, virus, the body”, courtesy of Charis Tsevis. Viral latency and reactivation images, courtesy of Alamy Stock Photo. Figure created with BioRender.com (accessed on 18 February 2022).</p></figcaption></figure><p>Early analyses of the microbiome revealed a reduction in the number of nonpathogenic bacteria and an increase in the number of opportunistic pathogens in the nasal flora of astronauts [<a href="#B122-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B122-life-12-00495">122</a>]. Similarly, culture-based analyses conducted during the Skylab missions registered noteworthy elevations in counts of anaerobic bacteria, streptococci, Neisseria, lactobacilli and enteric bacilli in the oral microflora, in-flight compared to pre-flight samples from a total of 18 astronauts [<a href="#B90-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B90-life-12-00495">90</a>]; however, none of these changes were considered hazardous to astronauts’ health. These findings are consistent with the work conducted by Voorhies et al. (2019), discussed earlier, which showed that the microbiome composition of skin, nose and tongue, such as the gut microbiome, changes in microgravity, and additionally, becomes more similar between astronauts [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. </p>
<p>A more recent study used metagenomic sequencing to investigate the microbial profile of mouth, skin, nose, ear, and saliva swabs collected from an astronaut at eight different time points prior to, during and post-spaceflight [<a href="#B99-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-life-12-00495">99</a>]. While the main objective of the study was to determine the influence of the crew microbiome on the microbial composition of ISS habitable surfaces, it was noted that in saliva samples a flight-dependent decrease in species diversity was observed along with an increase in the relative abundance of <em>Alloprevotella</em> [<a href="#B99-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-life-12-00495">99</a>], a genus associated with dental caries [<a href="#B123-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B123-life-12-00495">123</a>]. Interestingly, an increase in the effective number of species was recorded in the samples after returning to Earth. </p>
<p>In a later study, shotgun metagenomic sequencing and microarrays were applied to characterize the microbial diversity of four astronauts, before, during and following spaceflight on the ISS [<a href="#B100-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100-life-12-00495">100</a>]. The authors reported that astronaut microbiome composition of body swabs and saliva samples changed during spaceflight but went back to normal post-flight. Moreover, these changes were not universal for all four crew members. Interestingly, the relative abundance of the genus <em>Prevotella</em> was found to be increased in the saliva samples of two astronauts. The genus consists of several common oral species and increased abundance has been linked to a diseased periodontal state [<a href="#B124-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B124-life-12-00495">124</a>]. Additional changes observed in the saliva samples concerned antimicrobial resistance genes: most notably, the elfamycin resistance gene significantly increased in all four astronauts following return to Earth, an aspect which should be considered when administrating antibiotic treatments post spaceflight.</p>
<p>In another recent study, Urbaniak et al. (2020) used 16S rRNA gene amplicon sequencing to monitor spaceflight-induced salivary microbiome changes [<a href="#B101-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101-life-12-00495">101</a>]. Based on the data on microbial composition and diversity, the authors suggested that astronauts’ microbiome can adapt to spaceflight conditions and, moreover, is less recalcitrant to microbiome effects during spaceflight upon re-exposure. Half of the participants involved in the study (i.e., 5) had distinct microbial communities pre-flight, in-flight, and post-flight. Quite interestingly, these subjects were on their first mission, while the other five subjects, who had previously flown to the ISS, did not display microbiome differences. <em>Streptococcus</em> was the most abundant organism in the saliva (i.e., 8% of the total organisms detected) and their diversity decreased during spaceflight.</p>
<p>Changes in the skin and nose microbiome were investigated in a study by Voorhies et al. (2019) [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>] that aimed at a thorough characterization of the microbiome’s fluctuation during 6- to 12-month space exploration. The forehead and forearm skin microbiota of 9 astronauts appeared to be differentially influenced by the ISS environment: diversity and richness increased or decreased depending upon the individual; however, it was consistent between the two sampled areas [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. Moreover, a common shift in the microbial composition was observed in all crew members and affected the abundance of <em>Proteobacteria</em>, mostly <em>Gammaproteobacteria</em> and <em>Betaproteobacteria</em>. A concomitant increase in <em>Firmicutes</em>, including the genera <em>Staphylococcus</em> and <em>Streptococcus</em>, was also observed. The authors speculated that decreased levels of <em>Gammaproteobacteria</em> may possibly be due to the lack of a “green” natural environment, the constant filtration of air and the alteration of the skin structure during spaceflight [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. As a reduction in skin <em>Gammaproteobacteria</em> has been associated with inflammation and allergy sensitization [<a href="#B125-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B125-life-12-00495">125</a>], it is possible that it also plays a role in the occurrence of skin hypersensitivity reactions, rashes, and skin infections, frequently observed in astronauts [<a href="#B126-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B126-life-12-00495">126</a>]. In this scenario, skin infections caused by opportunistic pathogens, such as the staphylococcal and streptococcal species, may be facilitated. Fewer spaceflight-dependent changes were found in the nose microbiome, as compared to the skin; however, they concerned the same bacterial genera, whose abundance was found to be modulated in skin (i.e., increase in <em>Staphylococcus</em>, <em>Corynebacterium-1</em> and <em>Bifidobacterium</em>). An elevated relative abundance of clinical pathogenic bacteria such as <em>Staphylococcus</em> in the nose has been reported by several studies in association with chronic rhinosinusitis, allergic rhinitis, and asthma [<a href="#B127-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B127-life-12-00495">127</a>,<a href="#B128-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128-life-12-00495">128</a>]. Hence, it is consistent with symptoms such as prolonged congestion, rhinitis, and sneezing, which have been reported by astronauts. Nonetheless, other factors could also be playing a role in it. Many of the observed changes in the nose microbiome persisted for at least 2 months after the astronauts returned to Earth. </p>
<p>Increased abundance of <em>Staphylococcus</em> in the nasal cavity has also been reported in a recent study examining temporal characteristics of the oropharyngeal and nasal microbiome during a 180-day ground-based confined experiment in the Controlled Ecological Life Support System (CELSS) [<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>]. 16S rDNA high-throughput sequencing was used to analyze data from four volunteers at eight time points during confinement and the results showed that the structure of the oropharyngeal and nasal microbiota varied greatly. Individual differences were also observed, with bacterial community structure and diversity changing with time. As with <em>Staphylococcus</em> in the nasal cavity, the abundance of <em>Neisseria</em> increased over time in the oropharynx. <em>Staphylococcus</em> in particular showed the characteristics of inter-individual transfer, suggesting that the microbiota structure and health of the respiratory tract could be affected by living in a closed environment for a long time.</p>
<p>To date, studies have revealed modifications in the skin, oral and nasal microbiome as a consequence of both spaceflight conditions and confinement in ground-based experiments. Changes at the individual level, and specifically an increase in a microbiome’s richness and diversity—i.e., alpha diversity—were reported during spaceflight in studies of the saliva microbiome [<a href="#B100-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100-life-12-00495">100</a>,<a href="#B101-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101-life-12-00495">101</a>], but changes at the populational level were not detected. By contrast, a decrease in alpha diversity in in-flight saliva samples was indicated by an earlier report [<a href="#B99-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-life-12-00495">99</a>], but this trend was not consistent across all four astronauts involved in the study. Mixed responses were detected in nasal and skin samples [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>]. Individual differences in the reaction of the skin microbiota to spaceflight may be attributed to the composition of the microbial communities, but also to skin-specific properties such as moisture and pH and/or astronauts’ personal hygiene habits [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. Concerning the nasal microbiome, changes were observed in relation to both spaceflight [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>] and ground-based confined experiments [<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>], suggesting that the nasal flora of all crewmembers may evolve in the same direction. Microbial transfer between individuals could play a role in it, suggesting that the microbiota structure and health of the respiratory tract could be affected by living in a closed environment for a long time [<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>]. Aspects including microbial interactions and exchange of microbiota within the crew or with the environment have not yet been fully elucidated and further studies will therefore need to be conducted.</p></section></section><section id="sec3dot3-life-12-00495"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.3. Impact of Spaceflight Conditions on Microbial Physiology and Host-Microbe Interactions</h3>
<p>Space microbiology studies have suggested that microgravity is a dominant factor influencing bacterial growth kinetics and cell behavior, and that space radiation may be responsible for increased mutation rates in microbes [<a href="#B129-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B129-life-12-00495">129</a>]. Several in vitro studies with bacteria have reported significant in-flight responses that included increased growth rate and cell concentration [<a href="#B130-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B130-life-12-00495">130</a>]. Other studies indicated that microgravity is associated with changes in gene expression and virulence factors and can promote antibiotic resistance and elevated transfer rates of genetic material between cells [<a href="#B131-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B131-life-12-00495">131</a>,<a href="#B132-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B132-life-12-00495">132</a>]. Microgravity-induced changes also include increased membrane integrity and differential secondary metabolite production [<a href="#B133-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B133-life-12-00495">133</a>]. </p>
<p>Decreased susceptibility of bacterial pathogens to antimicrobial agents has been repeatedly observed during space missions (e.g., Cytos 2 experiment, Antibio experiment during the Spacelab D1 mission, Space Shuttle STS-42 mission, etc.) and in ground-based simulations using model organisms such as <em>Escherichia coli</em> and <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em> [<a href="#B134-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B134-life-12-00495">134</a>]. These finding show that in space, bacteria adapt to grow at higher antibiotic concentrations, compared to ground samples [<a href="#B135-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B135-life-12-00495">135</a>]. Similarly, resistance to a broad range of antibiotics (e.g., chloramphenicol and cefalotin, persisting for over 110 generations) [<a href="#B136-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B136-life-12-00495">136</a>], as well as increased production of the heat-labile enterotoxin [<a href="#B137-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B137-life-12-00495">137</a>] and enhanced adherence to mammalian gastrointestinal epithelium [<a href="#B138-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B138-life-12-00495">138</a>], have been observed in <em>E. coli</em> under simulated microgravity (SMG) conditions.</p>
<p>Moreover, increased virulence and resistance to environmental stress were observed in cultures of the pathogenic bacterium <em>Salmonella typhimurium</em> grown under spaceflight conditions, together with increased survival in murine macrophages following oral infection [<a href="#B139-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B139-life-12-00495">139</a>]. The mechanisms contributing to this enhanced virulence were the Hfq pathway, which is required for virulence in several bacterial pathogens and considered a global regulator of the microbial response to spaceflight [<a href="#B140-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B140-life-12-00495">140</a>], and extracellular matrix accumulation, which is part of biofilm formation. Biofilms protect bacteria from various environmental conditions and increased production of biofilm communities has been frequently observed in bacteria exposed to both simulated and real space conditions [<a href="#B134-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B134-life-12-00495">134</a>,<a href="#B141-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B141-life-12-00495">141</a>]. In <em>Candida albicans</em>, such a feature was found in combination with increased filamentation and increased amphotericin B resistance [<a href="#B142-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B142-life-12-00495">142</a>]. In spaceflight-grown bacteria (e.g., the opportunistic pathogen <em>Pseudomonas aeruginosa</em>), biofilms tend to show enhanced resistance towards disinfectants, antibiotics and environmental stresses [<a href="#B143-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B143-life-12-00495">143</a>]. Still in <em>P. aeruginosa</em> and in few other bacterial species, microgravity appears to also stimulate production of signaling molecules (e.g., <em>N-Acyl homoserine lactone</em>) able to trigger bacterial communication and to regulate virulence [<a href="#B144-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B144-life-12-00495">144</a>].</p>
<p>Considering the effects of spaceflight-dependent alterations on bacterial mono-cultures, the impact of spaceflight on host-associated microbial communities can potentially be more profound. It is known that enhanced virulence of potential pathogens, immune system dysregulation, dysbiosis of the gut microbiome, and disruption of mutualistic interactions, can all be induced by microgravity [<a href="#B79-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-life-12-00495">79</a>,<a href="#B137-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B137-life-12-00495">137</a>,<a href="#B145-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B145-life-12-00495">145</a>]; however, the effects of prolonged exposure to microgravity—or various gravitational shifts (i.e., hypo- or hypergavity) that astronauts will face during deep-spaceflight missions—on the complex relationship between host and its microbiome are yet to be fully elucidated. </p>
<p>Microbe–animal interactions, e.g., mutualistic, pathogenic/parasitic or commensal, and functional networks of microbe–microbe can be equally affected by gravities encountered beyond LEO, with consequences for host health and wellbeing. These interactions rely on a complex system of communication between each member of a microbial community, that influences growth, physiology and metabolism [<a href="#B146-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B146-life-12-00495">146</a>]. One such example is horizontal gene transfer (HGT), which is the transfer of genetic material between microorganisms, through either transformation, conjugation or transduction. Research has shown that the human microbiome is a hotspot of HGT [<a href="#B147-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B147-life-12-00495">147</a>,<a href="#B148-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B148-life-12-00495">148</a>,<a href="#B149-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B149-life-12-00495">149</a>], with the rate of HGT between human-associated bacteria 25-fold higher than that among ecologically diverse non-human isolates [<a href="#B150-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B150-life-12-00495">150</a>]. HGT is significant as it regulates the exchange of antimicrobial-resistant (AMR) and virulence genes and is the main mechanism driving antibiotic resistance in bacterial communities [<a href="#B151-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B151-life-12-00495">151</a>]. Spaceflight conditions reportedly influence gene transfer and enhance HGT. For example, the content of mobile genetic elements appears to be higher in the genome of bacterial isolates grown in space (ISS) than in isolates of the same species from extreme built environments on Earth [<a href="#B152-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B152-life-12-00495">152</a>]. In line with this, HGT activity concerning the transfer of AMR genes was found to be more increased in bacteria under simulated microgravity conditions than in 1 G controls [<a href="#B80-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-life-12-00495">80</a>]. The transfer of these genes from donor (i.e., <em>Acinetobacter pitti</em>) to co-cultured recipient strains of <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em>, resulted in a phenotypic change, as the recipient strains developed resistance to the antibiotic oxacillin, which they were previously susceptible to [<a href="#B80-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-life-12-00495">80</a>]. A similar study conducted on the ISS showed increased gene transfer as well, using a phenomenological model. However, plasmid stability was short lived, resulting in overall lower antibiotic resistance compared to ground controls [<a href="#B153-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B153-life-12-00495">153</a>]. According to the authors, the reason lies in the fact that processes of segregation and spontaneous elimination of drug resistance genes would prevail over the processes of their cointegration [<a href="#B153-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B153-life-12-00495">153</a>]. The exact mechanism influencing enhanced HGT is yet to be determined; nonetheless, it may be related to increased competence or transduction, known to be promoted in bacteria in response to different stressors [<a href="#B154-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B154-life-12-00495">154</a>]. Regardless of the mechanisms, the potential impact of the space environment in promoting increased spread of determinants associated with antibiotic resistance and virulence, is of particular concern. Furthermore, risks are not restricted to the astronauts’ microbiome but may as well arise within the microbial flora populating the spacecraft environment (air, surface, water, etc.).</p>
<p>Acquired antibiotic resistance can have serious implications as the efficacy of antibiotic treatment may be diminished during space missions [<a href="#B129-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B129-life-12-00495">129</a>]. Although antibiotic resistance may also increase during short-term spaceflights [<a href="#B155-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B155-life-12-00495">155</a>], in the context of long-term space missions, strategy and tactics of using antibiotics would have particular relevance. Over-prescribed antibiotic therapy can generally represent an infectious risk factor, potentially resulting in accumulation of antibiotic resistance and pathogenic genes on certain strains due to the process of plasmid mobilization and cointegration [<a href="#B153-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B153-life-12-00495">153</a>]. In confined compartments such as spacecrafts, this issue is of even greater concern, not only since microbial communities are a pool for the propagation of antibiotic resistance genes, but also because the transmission of a resistant strain among individuals would be facilitated [<a href="#B79-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-life-12-00495">79</a>]. Under these conditions, the formation and spread of multidrug-resistant strains of microorganisms and of strains with increased virulence (disease-causing microbes) could be promoted, which, in conjunction with dysbiosis and a possible weakened immune system during spaceflight, carries the risk of increased severity of crewmembers’ infection in long-term space missions [<a href="#B156-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B156-life-12-00495">156</a>]. </p>
<p>More studies will be needed to further our understanding of space environment-driven microbiome changes during long missions, to clarify whether the effects on communities are potentiated or dampened and what the consequences are for the host. In order to obtain a realistic overview of host–microbiome relationships, investigations should not be limited to disease-causing microbes that represent a potential risk to astronauts but shall also include mutualistic and commensal microbes. This can be crucial to learn what drives microbial fitness in the spaceflight environment and how to maintain a healthy symbiotic homeostasis [<a href="#B133-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B133-life-12-00495">133</a>].</p></section><section id="sec3dot4-life-12-00495"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.4. Influence of the Space Dietary Regime on the Microbiome</h3>
<p>Food intake is strictly connected to the composition and the functionality of the microbiome. Research has shown that changes in the diet are followed by rearrangement of the gut flora that can occur within just 24 h of initiation and have secondary effects on host immunologic and metabolic markers [<a href="#B157-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B157-life-12-00495">157</a>]. As such, protein consumption positively correlates with overall microbial diversity, with animal protein intake leading to increased abundance of bile-tolerant organisms such as <em>Bacteroides</em>, <em>Alistipes</em>, and <em>Bilophila</em> [<a href="#B158-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B158-life-12-00495">158</a>], whereas a diet rich in fibers tends to increase the abundance of bifidobacteria and lactic acid bacteria, known for their anti-inflammatory properties [<a href="#B118-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B118-life-12-00495">118</a>]. Along with the type of food, the food’s form (raw or cooked) appears to influence diet-driven host–microbial interactions, with cooking impacting the gut microbiome differently on meat versus plant-based (e.g., tuber) diets [<a href="#B159-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B159-life-12-00495">159</a>]. Additionally, multiple compounds with known antimicrobial effects are significantly decreased in cooked food, thus limiting their bioactivity [<a href="#B160-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B160-life-12-00495">160</a>]. The close relationship between diet, the gut microbiome, and health suggests that modulations in the diet can have a beneficial or detrimental impact on our health, depending on the relative identity and abundance of constituent bacterial population [<a href="#B161-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B161-life-12-00495">161</a>].</p>
<p>It follows that a change in diet such as that faced by astronauts, can also potentially affect the gut microbiome with repercussions on their health [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. Space food includes a variety of products specifically created for the consumption in outer space and as such, it must meet certain criteria: nutritional properties in line with the crew’s recommended caloric intake, palatability, ease of preparation and storage, etc. [<a href="#B67-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-life-12-00495">67</a>]. Since water, storage, crew time, and food preparation capability—which does not include cooking—are limited, current space food consists of a narrow choice of shelf-stable, single-serving food products either in their natural form or preserved by dehydration, retort thermostabilization, or irradiation [<a href="#B162-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B162-life-12-00495">162</a>]. The variety and amount of fresh fruit and vegetables vary, and their consumption is limited to a few days or weeks.
