Hence, the ISS food system, for instance, is dominated by meat and meat products [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>] and long-term missions will have to rely on bioregenerative life support systems (BLSSs)—e.g., integrating plant cultivation—to introduce a larger variety of food [<a href="#B71-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71-life-12-00495">71</a>].</p>
<p>The optimization of the food system to mitigate negative effects of spaceflight on crewmembers’ health and performance is paramount. A reduction in the astronauts’ caloric intake to 70–80% of the daily requirement [<a href="#B163-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B163-life-12-00495">163</a>] occurs at the beginning of the mission due to space motion sickness; however, it can extend well beyond the first days of flight [<a href="#B164-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B164-life-12-00495">164</a>], potentially leading to the loss of both fat and lean tissue mass, and to adverse effects on muscle, bone and cardiovascular systems and motor and cognitive functions [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>,<a href="#B165-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B165-life-12-00495">165</a>]. Moreover, although the reasons are not entirely clear, insulin resistance and glucose intolerance are frequently observed both in short- and longer-term space missions, and in analog missions [<a href="#B166-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B166-life-12-00495">166</a>]. A reduced caloric intake like that often experienced by astronauts, may also lead to a restructuring of the gut microbiome similar to that observed in association with very-low-calorie diet (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>) [<a href="#B167-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B167-life-12-00495">167</a>]. Accordingly, a low calorie intake and the consequent weight loss may be paralleled by a decrease in bacterial abundance, impaired nutrient absorption, and enrichment in endogenous enteric pathogens (e.g., <em>Clostridioides difficile</em>), suggesting that diet-induced shifts in the gut microbiome may influence colonization resistance and thus host physiology. Low-caloric intake may additionally be associated with increased inflammation and oxidative stress, with possible repercussions on the functioning of the immune system [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>]. Countermeasures have been developed to provide more balanced diets with increased average caloric intake, optimized to reduce nutrient deficiency and to improve energy supply to lessen the potential downstream dysregulation of the immune system [<a href="#B168-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B168-life-12-00495">168</a>]. Despite these efforts, however, diet imbalance during spaceflight remains an issue, as it depends on multiple factors that are not limited to food quality ad palatability but also include cultural habits, alteration of circadian rhythms and in general, the stress associated with the mission [<a href="#B169-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B169-life-12-00495">169</a>]. </p>
<p>Notwithstanding individual differences, microbiome studies carried out in both real and analog missions generally reported rearrangements in the gut microbiome consistent with the higher abundance of bacteria associated with chronic intestinal inflammation and a concomitant reduction in the number of genera with known anti-inflammatory properties [<a href="#B89-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-life-12-00495">89</a>,<a href="#B94-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94-life-12-00495">94</a>,<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. In some cases, these data correlated with a moderate increase in the inflammatory immune response observed in the crew during spaceflight [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. Such changes in relative abundance of gut microbial components are possibly the result of multiple factors connected to spaceflight. However, evidence demonstrated the impact of the diet—i.e., a plant- and fiber-based BLSS-dietary structure—on the maintenance of a high gut microbiome diversity enriched with bacteria having anti-inflammatory action during analog missions [<a href="#B95-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B95-life-12-00495">95</a>]. Production of SCFAs through gut microbiome-mediated fermentation of non-digestible carbohydrates was shown to contribute, among other things, to the maintenance of metabolic homeostasis [<a href="#B170-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-life-12-00495">170</a>]. </p>
<p>Providing the crewmembers with a balanced diet, possibly delivered through BLSSs and rich in fibers, is therefore of great importance to help prevent nutritional imbalances as well as to preserve a healthy gut microbiome. Due to individual differences, personalized nutritional approaches have been suggested [<a href="#B171-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171-life-12-00495">171</a>]. Furthermore, probiotics-based countermeasures via the supplementation of given bacteria strains (e.g., <em>Akkermansia, Bifidobacterium</em>, etc.) [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B172-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-life-12-00495">172</a>,<a href="#B173-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-life-12-00495">173</a>] or the administration of SCFA-producing next-generation probiotics (<em>Faecalibacterium</em>, <em>Roseburia</em>, etc.) [<a href="#B174-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B174-life-12-00495">174</a>], have additionally been proposed. </p></section><section id="sec3dot5-life-12-00495"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.5. Microbiome and Crewmembers Mental and Physical Health</h3>
<p>As we have emphasized throughout the review, the human microbiome contributes to overall health through different routes, including protection against pathogens, maintenance of the immune system, proper intestinal function, and contribution to metabolic functions [<a href="#B79-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-life-12-00495">79</a>,<a href="#B106-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B106-life-12-00495">106</a>,<a href="#B175-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-life-12-00495">175</a>]. Competitive exclusion of pathogenic bacteria is one of the main functions exerted by the microbiome communities residing in various anatomic sites of the human body. Antagonistic interactions play a pivotal role in determining the composition of a functional antimicrobial barrier, by means of strategies that include production of antibiotics, secretion of digestive enzymes, and quorum sensing [<a href="#B176-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-life-12-00495">176</a>]. Accordingly, the establishment of new microbes can be prevented through competition for shared nutrients and other resources, with endogenous bacteria [<a href="#B177-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B177-life-12-00495">177</a>]. Moreover, microbial communities help strengthen mucosal barrier function and can stimulate epithelial cells to produce antimicrobial peptides and proteins (e.g., bacteriocins), thereby killing pathogenic competitors and preventing translocation [<a href="#B173-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-life-12-00495">173</a>,<a href="#B178-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B178-life-12-00495">178</a>,<a href="#B179-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B179-life-12-00495">179</a>]. In view of this, particular concern has been generated by the documented changes in the astronauts’ microbiome [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>,<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B100-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100-life-12-00495">100</a>,<a href="#B101-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101-life-12-00495">101</a>] and their potential effects on astronaut health and performance, in conjunction with immune system dysregulations and increased risk of pathogenic infections during spaceflight [<a href="#B103-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-life-12-00495">103</a>,<a href="#B180-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-life-12-00495">180</a>]—e.g., transient or permanent governance of pathogenic/opportunistic bacterial species, e.g., <em>Staphylococcus</em> spp. [<a href="#B181-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B181-life-12-00495">181</a>] along with enhanced HGT and decreased susceptibility of pathogens to antimicrobial agents [<a href="#B80-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-life-12-00495">80</a>]. As a matter of fact, crewmembers do experience adverse medical events of varying severity during spaceflight missions, related to infectious diseases, which include cold sores, skin and urinary tract infections, lymphadenitis and pharyngitis [<a href="#B182-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-life-12-00495">182</a>]. </p>
<p>An altered microbiome is not only associated with the onset of infections but with non-infectious diseases as well, such as inflammatory bowel disease [<a href="#B183-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-life-12-00495">183</a>], systemic metabolic disorder (e.g., type 2 diabetes and obesity) and allergic reactions and sensitivities [<a href="#B184-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B184-life-12-00495">184</a>,<a href="#B185-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185-life-12-00495">185</a>]. Changes in the oropharingeal and in the skin microbiome are implicated in the development and progression of caries, gingivitis, tooth decay, as well as endocarditis and heart disease [<a href="#B186-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B186-life-12-00495">186</a>,<a href="#B187-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B187-life-12-00495">187</a>,<a href="#B188-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B188-life-12-00495">188</a>,<a href="#B189-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B189-life-12-00495">189</a>], pharyngitis, asthma and pneumonia [<a href="#B190-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B190-life-12-00495">190</a>,<a href="#B191-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B191-life-12-00495">191</a>,<a href="#B192-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B192-life-12-00495">192</a>], acne, atopic dermatitis, psoriasis and chronic wound pathology [<a href="#B193-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B193-life-12-00495">193</a>,<a href="#B194-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B194-life-12-00495">194</a>]. Moreover, evidence of microbial dysbiosis has been observed in conjunction with various types of cancers [<a href="#B195-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B195-life-12-00495">195</a>], including colorectal cancer (CRC) [<a href="#B30-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-life-12-00495">30</a>,<a href="#B196-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B196-life-12-00495">196</a>] and breast cancer [<a href="#B31-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-life-12-00495">31</a>], among others. The space environment has been shown to alter the tumor microenvironment and promote tumor cell proliferation, transformation and survival [<a href="#B197-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B197-life-12-00495">197</a>]. Indeed, US astronauts have an increased incidence of prostate cancer and melanoma, similar to that observed with airline pilots, compared to the general US population [<a href="#B198-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B198-life-12-00495">198</a>]. Considering the role that an altered microbiome plays in cancer development and/or progression, microbial disruption during spaceflight, coupled with higher radiation exposure [<a href="#B55-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-life-12-00495">55</a>], could put astronauts at an even greater risk of developing certain types of cancers with long-duration missions.</p>
<p>In addition to the aforementioned functions of the microbiome in supporting host physiology, research has more recently shed light on the relationship between the gut microbiome and mental health through what is known as the brain–gut–microbiome axis (BGMA) [<a href="#B199-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B199-life-12-00495">199</a>]. BGMA signaling has been suggested to be bi-directional, as not only can gut bacteria influence health and the development of emotional behavior, but psychological states can in turn alter gut health [<a href="#B200-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B200-life-12-00495">200</a>]. Moreover, the communication appears to occur directly and indirectly via the central and enteric nervous systems and the vagus nerve, through endocrine, neural and immune pathways [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. Microbiota–gut–brain communication has hitherto mostly been explored in animal models, with human research lagging behind. However, studies have indicated that the gut microbiota can modulate the BGMA via multiple mechanisms, including alterations in microbial composition (i.e., SCFA-producing bacteria seem to be associated with higher mental quality of life indicators [<a href="#B201-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B201-life-12-00495">201</a>]) or the potential production of microbial neuroactive metabolites (e.g., SCFAs, neurotransmitters, hormones or neurotoxic metabolites) [<a href="#B202-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B202-life-12-00495">202</a>,<a href="#B203-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B203-life-12-00495">203</a>]. Preliminary studies have also demonstrated gut microbiota alterations in subjects affected by neurodevelopment disorders such as schizophrenia and autism spectrum disorders [<a href="#B204-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B204-life-12-00495">204</a>,<a href="#B205-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B205-life-12-00495">205</a>]. Furthermore, perturbations of the BGMA have been found in association with stress-related gastrointestinal disorders [<a href="#B206-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B206-life-12-00495">206</a>], as well as increased anxiety [<a href="#B207-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B207-life-12-00495">207</a>], depression [<a href="#B208-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B208-life-12-00495">208</a>], Parkinson’s disease [<a href="#B209-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B209-life-12-00495">209</a>] and decreased cognitive abilities [<a href="#B203-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B203-life-12-00495">203</a>], suggesting that the microbiota may contribute to shaping cognitive networks encompassing emotional and social domains [<a href="#B205-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B205-life-12-00495">205</a>]. Of interest, altered BGMA and microbiome have also been found in conjunction with disrupted sleep physiology, with microbiome diversity being positively correlated with increased sleep efficiency and total sleep time [<a href="#B200-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B200-life-12-00495">200</a>]. Cognitive or behavioral conditions reported during spaceflight include reduced sleep quality and increased anxiety and depression, which can be accompanied by the impairment of psychomotor functions and neurocognitive performance [<a href="#B210-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B210-life-12-00495">210</a>,<a href="#B211-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B211-life-12-00495">211</a>]. Occurring as a result of several mission-related environmental (e.g., radiation, microgravity, excessive exposure to noise and light) and psychosocial stressors (e.g., isolation, homesickness), these symptoms represent a threat to the success of space missions, as they greatly affect astronaut wellbeing [<a href="#B212-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B212-life-12-00495">212</a>]. In light of the bidirectional interactions between the gut microbiome and the brain [<a href="#B213-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B213-life-12-00495">213</a>], based on which the microbiome can influence cognition and emotion, it can be assumed that strategies aimed at maintaining a healthy microbiome might also be helpful in mitigating unwanted neurobehavioral effects [<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. In line with this, research shows that successful treatment of anxiety symptoms can be achieved by regulation of intestinal microbiota by means of both probiotic and non-probiotic (e.g., regulating diet, supplementation of short-chain fructooligosaccharides scFOS) interventions [<a href="#B214-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B214-life-12-00495">214</a>]. This aspect is of particular significance as it underlines the far-reaching impact of the gut microbiome and offers new perspectives regarding the understanding and at the same time the mitigation of psychological stressors (external or self-imposed) that may arise during spaceflight.</p>
<p>Although microbiome changes generally seem to rebound after returning to Earth [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>], the entity and persistence of alterations induced by longer space missions (e.g., deep-space missions) and the associated risk of increased severity of infection, disease onset, and mental health impairments, are yet to be fully assessed. In the long run, compositional changes in the gut flora might even predispose astronauts to more prolonged-development diseases such as IBS, autoimmunity and even cancer [<a href="#B182-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-life-12-00495">182</a>]. The risks associated with these factors should therefore be explored in more depth.</p></section></section><section id="sec4-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">4. Spaceflight, Microbiome and Immunity </h2>
<p>Understanding the combined effects of spaceflight on the immune system is significant for the health and safety of crewmembers and is an important area of interest as long-term exploration flights become more common. The intense conditions that astronauts encounter in space, including radiation exposure, microgravity, changes in diet, disruption of circadian rhythm and stress, all have an effect on the immune system (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>) [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>,<a href="#B215-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B215-life-12-00495">215</a>,<a href="#B216-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B216-life-12-00495">216</a>,<a href="#B217-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B217-life-12-00495">217</a>,<a href="#B218-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B218-life-12-00495">218</a>,<a href="#B219-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B219-life-12-00495">219</a>,<a href="#B220-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B220-life-12-00495">220</a>]. A dysregulated immune response has been well-established during spaceflight, with many changes to immune cell parameters, such as in the distribution, function and proliferation of leukocytes [<a href="#B215-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B215-life-12-00495">215</a>,<a href="#B216-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B216-life-12-00495">216</a>,<a href="#B221-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B221-life-12-00495">221</a>,<a href="#B222-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B222-life-12-00495">222</a>,<a href="#B223-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B223-life-12-00495">223</a>,<a href="#B224-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B224-life-12-00495">224</a>,<a href="#B225-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B225-life-12-00495">225</a>,<a href="#B226-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B226-life-12-00495">226</a>,<a href="#B227-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B227-life-12-00495">227</a>,<a href="#B228-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B228-life-12-00495">228</a>,<a href="#B229-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-life-12-00495">229</a>], cytokine profiles [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>,<a href="#B217-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B217-life-12-00495">217</a>,<a href="#B219-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B219-life-12-00495">219</a>,<a href="#B221-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B221-life-12-00495">221</a>,<a href="#B222-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B222-life-12-00495">222</a>,<a href="#B229-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-life-12-00495">229</a>,<a href="#B230-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B230-life-12-00495">230</a>,<a href="#B231-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B231-life-12-00495">231</a>,<a href="#B232-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B232-life-12-00495">232</a>,<a href="#B233-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B233-life-12-00495">233</a>] and neutrophil [<a href="#B221-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B221-life-12-00495">221</a>,<a href="#B234-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B234-life-12-00495">234</a>], monocyte [<a href="#B232-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B232-life-12-00495">232</a>,<a href="#B235-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B235-life-12-00495">235</a>] and NK cell function [<a href="#B236-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B236-life-12-00495">236</a>,<a href="#B237-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B237-life-12-00495">237</a>,<a href="#B238-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B238-life-12-00495">238</a>]. As discussed earlier, microbial physiology changes as a result of spaceflight, with one such change being increased virulence in some pathogens grown in space [<a href="#B139-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B139-life-12-00495">139</a>,<a href="#B144-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B144-life-12-00495">144</a>,<a href="#B239-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B239-life-12-00495">239</a>,<a href="#B240-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B240-life-12-00495">240</a>], which is problematic considering the dysregulated immune responses astronauts experience during spaceflight [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>,<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>]. However, the observation that a vaccine administered in space was able to stimulate an appropriate immune response is promising for long-term missions [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>]. The effect of gut microbiome dysbiosis is an essential factor to consider when reflecting on the immunity of astronauts as the microbiome has essential roles in the development and function of both the innate immune system—including regulating neutrophils [<a href="#B241-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B241-life-12-00495">241</a>], and macrophages [<a href="#B242-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B242-life-12-00495">242</a>,<a href="#B243-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B243-life-12-00495">243</a>], and the adaptive immune system—including influence on the function and repertoire of B cells [<a href="#B244-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B244-life-12-00495">244</a>], the induction of intestinal IgA [<a href="#B245-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B245-life-12-00495">245</a>], the differentiation of Tfh cells [<a href="#B246-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B246-life-12-00495">246</a>,<a href="#B247-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B247-life-12-00495">247</a>], and transition of antigen-activated CD8 T cells into memory cells [<a href="#B248-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B248-life-12-00495">248</a>]. Although there are variations in the results due to different circumstances such as spaceflight duration, sample retrieval and experimental protocols, overall, the generation, function or proportion of immune cells are affected during spaceflight, which disrupts the homeostasis required for an appropriate immune response [<a href="#B97-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-life-12-00495">97</a>,<a href="#B222-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B222-life-12-00495">222</a>,<a href="#B228-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B228-life-12-00495">228</a>]. </p>
<p>Dysbiosis of the gut microbiome has been associated with a dysregulated immune system, where changes in T cell regulation and cytokine secretion are observed [<a href="#B249-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B249-life-12-00495">249</a>,<a href="#B250-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B250-life-12-00495">250</a>,<a href="#B251-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B251-life-12-00495">251</a>,<a href="#B252-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B252-life-12-00495">252</a>]. The gut microbiome has a significant role in differentiating naive CD4+ T cells, which defend against extracellular pathogens and suppress the immune system when a response is not required. Microbes such as <em>Bacteroides fragilis</em>, segmented filamentous bacteria and <em>Clostridia</em> can differentially induce the development of T<sub>H</sub>1, T<sub>H</sub>17 and T<sub>reg</sub> cells, respectively [<a href="#B19-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-life-12-00495">19</a>,<a href="#B250-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B250-life-12-00495">250</a>,<a href="#B253-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B253-life-12-00495">253</a>,<a href="#B254-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B254-life-12-00495">254</a>,<a href="#B255-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-life-12-00495">255</a>]. T cell function is affected in space, but the response differs depending on the flight duration, as short-term missions increase T cell function and long-term missions cause T cell function to decrease upon landing [<a href="#B229-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-life-12-00495">229</a>]. Cytokine production profiles, such as IFN𝛾, IL-17 and IL-10, are also affected during spaceflight [<a href="#B222-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B222-life-12-00495">222</a>,<a href="#B228-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B228-life-12-00495">228</a>]. These are cytokines that are secreted from immune cells, which are regulated by the gut microbiome [<a href="#B249-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B249-life-12-00495">249</a>,<a href="#B256-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B256-life-12-00495">256</a>,<a href="#B257-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B257-life-12-00495">257</a>]. Additionally, astronauts experience spaceflight-related reductions in certain SCFA-producing bacteria in their gut microbiome, such as <em>Pseudobutyrivibrio</em> and <em>Akkermansia</em> [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>], which may be a result of the imposed conditions of flight, such as the decrease in dietary fiber in the astronaut diet [<a href="#B6-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-life-12-00495">6</a>,<a href="#B258-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B258-life-12-00495">258</a>]. SCFAs produced by the gut microbiota are important in immune system regulation, as they have a role in CD4+ and CD8+ T cell function, generation and cytokine secretion [<a href="#B259-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B259-life-12-00495">259</a>,<a href="#B260-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B260-life-12-00495">260</a>]. Butyrate, for example, has been identified in reducing gastrointestinal inflammation through the induction of IL-10, inhibiting the secretion of pro-inflammatory cytokines, and regulating innate immune cells and T<sub>reg</sub> cells [<a href="#B261-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B261-life-12-00495">261</a>,<a href="#B262-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B262-life-12-00495">262</a>,<a href="#B263-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B263-life-12-00495">263</a>,<a href="#B264-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B264-life-12-00495">264</a>]. Although many other factors have been implicated in the dysregulation of the immune system in space, the microbiome’s effect cannot be discounted.</p>
<p>Many astronauts experience uncharacteristic allergies and skin rashes during spaceflight [<a href="#B182-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-life-12-00495">182</a>], with some requiring antihistamines or steroids to manage these reactions [<a href="#B265-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B265-life-12-00495">265</a>]. These hypersensitivities may result from a T<sub>H</sub>2 shift in the immune system, which has been observed to occur in spaceflight [<a href="#B229-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-life-12-00495">229</a>]. Significantly higher IL-10 levels than IFN𝛾, which suggest a shift towards a T<sub>H</sub>2 response, were also observed among astronauts upon landing [<a href="#B229-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-life-12-00495">229</a>]. Skin reactions in space could be associated with changes to the skin microbiome since decreases in Gram-negative <em>Proteobacteria</em>, which includes <em>Acinetobacter</em>, were documented in astronauts [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. <em>Acinetobacter</em> is a commensal skin microbe that can help maintain homeostasis by reducing inflammation, regulating the balance between T<sub>H</sub>1 and T<sub>H</sub>2 cells, and inducing IL-10 production [<a href="#B266-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B266-life-12-00495">266</a>,<a href="#B267-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B267-life-12-00495">267</a>]. The relative proportions of <em>Bacteroidetes</em>, <em>Actinobacteria</em> and <em>Firmicutes</em>, in particular staphylococcal and streptococcal species, increase in the skin microbiome during space flight [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>]. An increase in <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em> colonization has been seen in patients with atopic dermatitis on Earth [<a href="#B268-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B268-life-12-00495">268</a>,<a href="#B269-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B269-life-12-00495">269</a>]. These alterations to the skin microbiome may contribute to the overactive immune responses encountered in space and may contribute to conditions such as erythema, psoriasis, various types of dermatitis and delayed wound healing, which represent frequent skin problems during space missions [<a href="#B126-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B126-life-12-00495">126</a>,<a href="#B265-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B265-life-12-00495">265</a>,<a href="#B270-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B270-life-12-00495">270</a>,<a href="#B271-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B271-life-12-00495">271</a>]. </p>
<p>Increased and persistent reactivation of Epstein–Barr virus (EBV), varicella-zoster virus, herpes-simplex-1, and cytomegalovirus, four naturally occurring latent herpesviruses, have been reported by astronauts during both short- (10–16 days) and long (60–180 days)-duration missions, with viral titers and shedding increased with the length of the mission [<a href="#B272-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B272-life-12-00495">272</a>,<a href="#B273-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B273-life-12-00495">273</a>]. While a robust and competent immune system is necessary to maintain latency, as observed in spaceflight [<a href="#B231-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B231-life-12-00495">231</a>,<a href="#B274-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B274-life-12-00495">274</a>] and space analog studies [<a href="#B275-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B275-life-12-00495">275</a>,<a href="#B276-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B276-life-12-00495">276</a>], a dysbiotic microbiome could also be a contributing factor (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>). This could be through changes in microbiome-immune modulation, or through changes in bacterial–viral interactions. In the case of the latter, metabolites produced from the oral microbiome were shown to influence viral reactivation from latent human immunodeficiency virus, Kaposi’s sarcoma herpesvirus, and EBV, by activating viral promotors or causing epigenetic modifications of the viral genome [<a href="#B277-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B277-life-12-00495">277</a>,<a href="#B278-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B278-life-12-00495">278</a>,<a href="#B279-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B279-life-12-00495">279</a>,<a href="#B280-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B280-life-12-00495">280</a>]. Correlation analyses between the salivary microbiome and EBV titers, in astronaut saliva, revealed a strong positive correlation (suggestive of promotion) with <em>Gracilibacteria</em> and <em>Abiotrophia</em> and a negative correlation (suggestive of protection) with Oribacterium, <em>Veillonella</em>, and <em>Haemophilus</em> [<a href="#B101-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101-life-12-00495">101</a>]. <em>Veillonella</em> is one of the main hydrogen sulfide (H<sub>2</sub>S) producers in the oral cavity [<a href="#B281-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B281-life-12-00495">281</a>], a chemical that is also produced by the intestinal microbiome [<a href="#B282-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B282-life-12-00495">282</a>]. While H<sub>2</sub>S displays antiviral activity against pathogenic RNA viruses [<a href="#B283-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B283-life-12-00495">283</a>,<a href="#B284-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B284-life-12-00495">284</a>], it is also regarded as an endogenous regulator of both the innate and adaptive immune arms [<a href="#B285-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B285-life-12-00495">285</a>]. Research has shown that therapeutically administered doses of H<sub>2</sub>S can improve the function of various immune cells and protect them against dysfunction from various stressors (reviewed in [<a href="#B285-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B285-life-12-00495">285</a>]). Studies are currently limited regarding the role of the microbiome in latent viral reactivation, especially as it pertains to spaceflight. However, this is a topic that warrants further investigation to help reduce the risks and complications of viral infections in astronauts during long-duration missions. While it is important to note that many cases on the ISS are asymptomatic [<a href="#B286-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B286-life-12-00495">286</a>], with a lower incidence of reactivation in recent years attributed to better biomedical countermeasures [<a href="#B287-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B287-life-12-00495">287</a>], infections that do arise, under certain circumstances, can lead to shingles, mononucleosis, various types of cancers and different inflammatory diseases such as myocarditis and pancreatitis, all of which will be hard to treat in outer space. </p>
<p>Many immune responses and resulting medical issues encountered by astronauts during spaceflight could be linked to abnormal microbiomes, and further studies should be conducted to gain insight into the mechanisms of these microbiomes in human health and immunity. </p></section><section id="sec5-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">5. The Impact of the Built Environment on the Astronaut Microbiome</h2>
<p>The microbiome of the built environment is the collective of microbial inhabitants in human-constructed environments [<a href="#B288-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B288-life-12-00495">288</a>]. The indoor microbiome is infrastructurally unique and differs between hospitals, offices, classrooms, and homes influenced by variations in material design, ventilation, temperature, humidity, pressure, and occupants [<a href="#B289-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B289-life-12-00495">289</a>,<a href="#B290-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B290-life-12-00495">290</a>,<a href="#B291-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B291-life-12-00495">291</a>,<a href="#B292-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B292-life-12-00495">292</a>,<a href="#B293-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B293-life-12-00495">293</a>]. Humans leave behind a microbial footprint through shedding, exhalation, and waste, accounting for approximately 40% of the microbes found in buildings [<a href="#B294-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B294-life-12-00495">294</a>,<a href="#B295-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B295-life-12-00495">295</a>]. It has been shown that less urbanized and more confined environments with reduced outside contact are even more overshadowed by human-associated microbes [<a href="#B296-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B296-life-12-00495">296</a>]. The ISS is one such confined environment, a unique habitat where the only exchange with the “outside” comes from the turnover in crew members, cargo capsules and supplies, with crew members being the main source of the ISS built environment microbial community [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B99-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-life-12-00495">99</a>,<a href="#B297-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B297-life-12-00495">297</a>,<a href="#B298-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B298-life-12-00495">298</a>]. Of the most abundant microbes catalogued on the ISS are those pertaining to skin, respiratory and gastrointestinal tracts [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B99-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-life-12-00495">99</a>,<a href="#B299-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B299-life-12-00495">299</a>]. These include <em>Staphylococcus</em>, <em>Propionibacterium</em>, <em>Actinobacterium</em>, <em>Enterobacterium</em>, <em>Corynebacterium</em>, <em>Streptococcus</em>, <em>Acinetobacter</em>, and <em>Pantoea</em>, along with various bacteria belonging to the phylum Firmicutes [<a href="#B78-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B78-life-12-00495">78</a>,<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B297-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B297-life-12-00495">297</a>,<a href="#B299-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B299-life-12-00495">299</a>,<a href="#B300-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B300-life-12-00495">300</a>]. The ISS microbiome has also been observed to change over time in accordance with flight and exchange of crewmembers suggesting that temporal changes in the built environment may be due to different occupants on board [<a href="#B297-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B297-life-12-00495">297</a>]. Earth-based studies in the inflatable lunar/Mars habitat mimicked this trend where microbial communities during complete vacancy at day 0 differed from those seen at day 30 post human occupancy [<a href="#B301-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B301-life-12-00495">301</a>]. Although there is ample evidence that the human microbiome can influence the built environment, microbial transfer is not unidirectional. Early microbial studies in Russian astronauts aboard the Salyut and Mir orbital stations, revealed an interchange of gut microbiota between crew members [<a href="#B122-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B122-life-12-00495">122</a>,<a href="#B302-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B302-life-12-00495">302</a>]. With new metagenomic technologies, a direct transmission between ISS surfaces and the astronaut microbiome has been observed, through either single-nucleotide polymorphism, haplotype matching and/or genomic read recruiting [<a href="#B303-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B303-life-12-00495">303</a>,<a href="#B304-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B304-life-12-00495">304</a>]. This two-way microbial transfer between the ISS environment and the astronaut suggests ISS occupants can not only help build the microbiome of the ISS, but also uptake it as their own. </p>
<p>Microbial transfer between astronaut and the ISS environment, as well as astronaut to astronaut (via surfaces) can be hazardous due to the altered immunity astronauts encounter during spaceflight, increasing their susceptibility to infection from opportunistic pathogens (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>) [<a href="#B156-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B156-life-12-00495">156</a>,<a href="#B235-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B235-life-12-00495">235</a>,<a href="#B236-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B236-life-12-00495">236</a>,<a href="#B305-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B305-life-12-00495">305</a>,<a href="#B306-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B306-life-12-00495">306</a>,<a href="#B307-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B307-life-12-00495">307</a>]. Evidence of opportunistic pathogens identified on ISS surfaces include cultivable populations of <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em>, <em>Staphylococcus hominis</em>, <em>Staphylococcus haemolyticus</em>, <em>Platanthera conspicua</em>, <em>Acinetobacter pittii</em>, <em>Klebsiella quasipneumoniae</em>, and <em>Aspergillus fumigatus</em> [<a href="#B297-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B297-life-12-00495">297</a>]. Although the ISS and astronauts are strictly monitored to prevent risks from pathogenic infection, conjunctivitis, acute upper respiratory tract, and urinary tract infections have been reported by crew members of the ISS [<a href="#B308-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B308-life-12-00495">308</a>,<a href="#B309-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B309-life-12-00495">309</a>]. Microbial virulence and antimicrobial resistance of these opportunistic pathogens could be further increased by the stressors of space, making infection not only more likely, but possibly harder to treat [<a href="#B217-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B217-life-12-00495">217</a>,<a href="#B310-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B310-life-12-00495">310</a>,<a href="#B311-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B311-life-12-00495">311</a>,<a href="#B312-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B312-life-12-00495">312</a>,<a href="#B313-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B313-life-12-00495">313</a>]. For example, <em>Staphylococcus epidermidis</em> grown in space acquired mutations in the <em>rpoB</em> gene, heightening its resistance to rifampcin [<a href="#B314-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B314-life-12-00495">314</a>,<a href="#B315-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B315-life-12-00495">315</a>] and <em>Aspergillus fumigatus</em> isolated from the ISS was significantly more lethal to neutrophil-deficient zebrafish compared to Earth-based clinical isolates [<a href="#B240-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B240-life-12-00495">240</a>]. </p>
<p>Building material can affect microbiome diversity and pathogenesis as well. Materials with higher hygroscopicity and porosity tend to have higher microbiome diversity due to moisture accumulation and environmental protection [<a href="#B316-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B316-life-12-00495">316</a>,<a href="#B317-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B317-life-12-00495">317</a>,<a href="#B318-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B318-life-12-00495">318</a>,<a href="#B319-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B319-life-12-00495">319</a>]. A submerged analog habitat that simulates ISS confinement and pressure found that different niches between particle board surfaces (LDP) and glass/metal surfaces selected for different viable microbial communities, with microbes found on LDP surfaces having higher abundance of antimicrobial and virulence associated genes. This suggests that material type can not only affect microbial diversity, but also pathogenicity [<a href="#B318-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B318-life-12-00495">318</a>]. It has been hypothesized that increased virulence and AMR resistance in confined environments, with low microbial diversity, are a result of adaptations that help bacteria and fungi survive in these restricted conditions [<a href="#B320-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B320-life-12-00495">320</a>,<a href="#B321-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B321-life-12-00495">321</a>,<a href="#B322-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B322-life-12-00495">322</a>,<a href="#B323-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B323-life-12-00495">323</a>]. These genomic and metabolic changes that occur in confined environments could explain the many novel species that have been identified in various confined habitats [<a href="#B324-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B324-life-12-00495">324</a>,<a href="#B325-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B325-life-12-00495">325</a>,<a href="#B326-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B326-life-12-00495">326</a>,<a href="#B327-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B327-life-12-00495">327</a>,<a href="#B328-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B328-life-12-00495">328</a>]. Efforts toward design of spacecraft materials to mitigate pathogenic growth would benefit from the prevention of infection rather than relying on treatment after infection, with limited medical resources. Analysis of other highly controlled environments such as spacecraft assembly clean rooms and intensive care units revealed that microbes in controlled environments rely more on nitrogen, carbohydrate, and heightened drug metabolisms versus in uncontrolled environments where microbes depend more on oxygen and amino acid metabolisms [<a href="#B329-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B329-life-12-00495">329</a>,<a href="#B330-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B330-life-12-00495">330</a>]. Understanding how microbes adapt to utilize different resources in a controlled built environment can help provide insight in future spacecraft design.</p>
<p>Another consideration for future spacecraft design is the risk of biofilm formation which can affect astronaut health and spacecraft integrity. Biofilms are associated with a range of disease including cystic fibrosis, osteomyelitis, prostatitis, rhinosinusitis, otitis media, urinary tract infection, endocarditis, periodontitis, and infectious kidney stones [<a href="#B331-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B331-life-12-00495">331</a>,<a href="#B332-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B332-life-12-00495">332</a>] and are responsible for 80% of chronic and recurrent infections [<a href="#B331-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B331-life-12-00495">331</a>,<a href="#B333-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B333-life-12-00495">333</a>,<a href="#B334-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B334-life-12-00495">334</a>]. Biofilms also induce corrosion, lead to mechanical blockages, and decrease the effectiveness of heat transfer on ISS equipment [<a href="#B335-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B335-life-12-00495">335</a>,<a href="#B336-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B336-life-12-00495">336</a>,<a href="#B337-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B337-life-12-00495">337</a>,<a href="#B338-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B338-life-12-00495">338</a>,<a href="#B339-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B339-life-12-00495">339</a>], putting spacecraft integrity at risk and posing an indirect safety hazard to the crew. Biofilm forming microbes such as <em>Acinetobacter</em>, <em>Sphingomonas</em>, <em>Bacillus</em>, <em>Burkholderia</em>, <em>Corynebacterium</em>, <em>Klebsiella</em> and fungi <em>Penicillium</em>, <em>Aspergillus</em>, <em>Cryptococcus</em>, <em>Rhodotorula</em> have been found on the ISS [<a href="#B297-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B297-life-12-00495">297</a>], though it is important to note that almost all species of bacteria can form biofilms under certain conditions; hence, many more biofilm formers could be present on the ISS. </p>
<p>The astronaut microbiota heavily contributes to the built environment of the ISS. Once transferred from host to environment, stressors such as microgravity, radiation, and confinement can alter pathogenicity, making it an infection risk for crew members should it be transferred to them from the environment. It is therefore vital to continually monitor microbial pathogenesis on the ISS to avoid crew member infections and continue studying the built environment for optimization for future space travel.</p></section><section id="sec6-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">6. Microbiome and Bone Health </h2>
<p>Astronaut bone loss during space flight has been an unresolved medical concern since the 1970s. Pre- and post-flight measurements of bone density in astronauts quantified with absorptiometry and quantitative computed tomography have shown an overall bone loss rate of 1–1.5% per month with areas of the lumbar spine, pelvis, and lower limbs contributing most heavily to the decline in bone density (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>) [<a href="#B340-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B340-life-12-00495">340</a>,<a href="#B341-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B341-life-12-00495">341</a>,<a href="#B342-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B342-life-12-00495">342</a>,<a href="#B343-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B343-life-12-00495">343</a>,<a href="#B344-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B344-life-12-00495">344</a>,<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>]. Measurements of volumetric bone mineral density in astronaut tibias after spaceflight showed a 5-percentile reduction that is comparable to average bone loss occurring in men over twenty years and 6 times faster than the accelerated bone loss that is often seen in menopausal women [<a href="#B346-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B346-life-12-00495">346</a>]. These findings have made bone loss treatment and prevention a high priority for astronaut health and safety. </p>
<p>Bones undergo modeling during development and remodeling in later stages of life to form, replace, and remove bone [<a href="#B347-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B347-life-12-00495">347</a>]. Osteoblasts are derived from stromal cells of the bone marrow and are responsible for bone formation [<a href="#B348-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B348-life-12-00495">348</a>]. In contrast, osteoclasts that derive from the hematopoietic stem cells in the bone marrow, resorb bone during remodeling, making the equilibrium between the bone forming osteoblast and bone resorbing osteoclast vital to overall bone homeostasis [<a href="#B349-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B349-life-12-00495">349</a>,<a href="#B350-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B350-life-12-00495">350</a>]. Bone formation and resorption can be biochemically measured in the urine through quantification of proteins produced during formation, such as bone-specific alkaline phosphatase and osteocalcin, as well as peptides released during matrix degradation, such as hydoxyproline, collagen type I, pyridinoline, and deoxypyridinoline [<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>,<a href="#B351-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B351-life-12-00495">351</a>,<a href="#B352-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B352-life-12-00495">352</a>]. These biochemical assays have been conducted on astronaut urine before, during, and after spaceflight to reveal that bone resorption markers heavily increase during spaceflight with only a slow increase in bone formation markers [<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>,<a href="#B353-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B353-life-12-00495">353</a>,<a href="#B354-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B354-life-12-00495">354</a>,<a href="#B355-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B355-life-12-00495">355</a>,<a href="#B356-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B356-life-12-00495">356</a>,<a href="#B357-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B357-life-12-00495">357</a>,<a href="#B358-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B358-life-12-00495">358</a>,<a href="#B359-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B359-life-12-00495">359</a>,<a href="#B360-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B360-life-12-00495">360</a>,<a href="#B361-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B361-life-12-00495">361</a>,<a href="#B362-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B362-life-12-00495">362</a>]. Post-flight measurements showed exponential decreases in bone resorption markers, but only a linear increase in bone formation markers, keeping bone homeostasis out of equilibrium during and post flight [<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>,<a href="#B357-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B357-life-12-00495">357</a>,<a href="#B358-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B358-life-12-00495">358</a>,<a href="#B359-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B359-life-12-00495">359</a>,<a href="#B362-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B362-life-12-00495">362</a>,<a href="#B363-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B363-life-12-00495">363</a>]. Furthermore, trabecular bone, the sponge-like structure at the epiphyses of long bones involved in metabolic processes associated with bone turnover, was also reduced post-flight [<a href="#B364-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B364-life-12-00495">364</a>,<a href="#B365-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B365-life-12-00495">365</a>,<a href="#B366-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B366-life-12-00495">366</a>]. Compromised trabeculae can irreversibly damage bone structure altogether [<a href="#B367-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B367-life-12-00495">367</a>,<a href="#B368-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B368-life-12-00495">368</a>]. One hypothesis contributing to region-specific bone loss during spaceflight is the changes in mechanical loading induced by microgravity [<a href="#B369-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B369-life-12-00495">369</a>,<a href="#B370-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B370-life-12-00495">370</a>]. High mechanical loading zones are reduced to low mechanical loading in microgravity and may therefore also reduce bone for the lack of necessity in that region. Extensive resistive exercise regimes have been employed in astronauts to stimulate mechanical loading to these areas that have shown to reduce bone loss, but do not ameliorate the bone loss process [<a href="#B342-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B342-life-12-00495">342</a>,<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>,<a href="#B365-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B365-life-12-00495">365</a>,<a href="#B371-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B371-life-12-00495">371</a>,<a href="#B372-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B372-life-12-00495">372</a>,<a href="#B373-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B373-life-12-00495">373</a>]. It has also been pointed out by Stanichuk et al. that bone density changes were also found in areas of neutral mechanical loading such as the skull, suggesting that microgravity-induced changes in mechanical loading may not be the sole contributor to bone changes during spaceflight [<a href="#B345-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B345-life-12-00495">345</a>]. </p>
<p>With increased understanding of the influence of the microbiome to overall health, many studies have revealed that the gut microbiome can also specifically influence bone health. Proteins and SCFAs produced by the gut microbiome have been shown to promote bone formation [<a href="#B374-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B374-life-12-00495">374</a>]. Butyrate, a short-chain fatty acid produce by <em>Lactobacillus</em> of the gut microbiome, promotes bone formation through T cell signaling inducing differentiation of osteoblasts in the bone marrow [<a href="#B375-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B375-life-12-00495">375</a>,<a href="#B376-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B376-life-12-00495">376</a>,<a href="#B377-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B377-life-12-00495">377</a>,<a href="#B378-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B378-life-12-00495">378</a>]. The gut microbiome is also a rich source of vitamin K2, which is required for the activation of osteocalcin, a protein produced by osteoblasts during bone formation [<a href="#B379-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B379-life-12-00495">379</a>,<a href="#B380-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B380-life-12-00495">380</a>]. Antibiotic-induced gut microbiome dysbiosis dampens vitamin K2 synthesis and is associated with decreases in osteocalcin and bone strength [<a href="#B381-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B381-life-12-00495">381</a>]. Bone loss in models that promote bone resorption can be reduced through dietary supplementation of beneficial bacteria, such as <em>Lactobacillus reuteri</em> which protects against bone resorption in estrogen-deficient mice [<a href="#B382-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B382-life-12-00495">382</a>,<a href="#B383-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B383-life-12-00495">383</a>] and trabecular bone loss during antibiotic dysbiosis in mice [<a href="#B384-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B384-life-12-00495">384</a>,<a href="#B385-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B385-life-12-00495">385</a>]. </p>
<p>Gut microbiome dysbiosis has been linked to bone disease in humans with osteoporosis and osteopenia, where diseased patients showed higher microbiome diversity than their healthy counterparts, and the severity of bone loss is positively correlated with higher microbiome diversity [<a href="#B386-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B386-life-12-00495">386</a>,<a href="#B387-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B387-life-12-00495">387</a>]. Gut microbiome dysbiosis can lead to inflammation followed by intestinal permeability that allows the gut microbiome to enter circulation [<a href="#B388-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B388-life-12-00495">388</a>,<a href="#B389-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B389-life-12-00495">389</a>]. Microbes in circulation stimulate an immune response from immune cells that recognize lipopolysaccharides using Toll-like receptors. Once recognized, the immune cells then activate to produce cytokines that promote maturation of T<sub>H</sub>17 cells in the bone marrow which then stimulate osteoclastogenesis and bone resorption [<a href="#B390-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B390-life-12-00495">390</a>,<a href="#B391-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B391-life-12-00495">391</a>,<a href="#B392-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B392-life-12-00495">392</a>]. As we have mentioned, the stressors of spaceflight can stimulate astronaut microbiome dysbiosis [<a href="#B110-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B110-life-12-00495">110</a>,<a href="#B111-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B111-life-12-00495">111</a>,<a href="#B217-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B217-life-12-00495">217</a>,<a href="#B393-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B393-life-12-00495">393</a>,<a href="#B394-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B394-life-12-00495">394</a>]. One such change during spaceflight is the decrease in genera with anti-inflammatory properties in the gut microbiome and the increase in <em>Parasutterella</em>, known to be associated with chronic inflammation [<a href="#B98-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-life-12-00495">98</a>,<a href="#B115-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-life-12-00495">115</a>]. These microbial changes may be increasing intestinal inflammation, which signals the promotion of bone resorption. Additionally, it is worth noting that astronaut gut microbiomes have been reported to have an increased abundance in <em>Firmicutes</em> [<a href="#B81-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-life-12-00495">81</a>], a similar phenomenon seen in the gut microbiomes of patients with osteoporosis [<a href="#B374-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B374-life-12-00495">374</a>,<a href="#B387-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B387-life-12-00495">387</a>]. There is evidence of both astronaut bone loss and microbiome dysbiosis during spaceflight; however, research is lacking on a possible association between the two. Additional investigation on this relationship could provide easier methods of inflight bone loss treatment and prevention through dietary pre and probiotic supplementation. Combining microbiome symbiosis with exercise regimes that maintain mechanical loading may help diminish bone loss during space travel.</p></section><section id="sec7-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">7. Gender Differences </h2>
<p>As space exploration expands to include more long-term missions, the health and safety of both male and female astronauts are important factors to consider. As there are many differences between the sexes, including the composition of their microbiomes, making sure that these differences are identified and evaluated is critical for understanding the impact spaceflight has on crewmembers. Microbiome diversity and composition diverge at similar ages after puberty in males and females, and high testosterone or estradiol levels result in a more diverse gut microbiome [<a href="#B395-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B395-life-12-00495">395</a>,<a href="#B396-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B396-life-12-00495">396</a>,<a href="#B397-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B397-life-12-00495">397</a>]. Studies have shown that microbes in the intestinal tract can impact sex hormone levels, and sex hormones have a role in shaping the gut microbiome composition [<a href="#B396-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B396-life-12-00495">396</a>,<a href="#B397-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B397-life-12-00495">397</a>,<a href="#B398-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B398-life-12-00495">398</a>,<a href="#B399-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B399-life-12-00495">399</a>,<a href="#B400-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B400-life-12-00495">400</a>,<a href="#B401-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B401-life-12-00495">401</a>,<a href="#B402-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B402-life-12-00495">402</a>,<a href="#B403-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B403-life-12-00495">403</a>]. This bi-directional relationship contributes to the gender-specific differences observed in disease, such as a greater occurrence of CaOx kidney stones [<a href="#B404-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B404-life-12-00495">404</a>,<a href="#B405-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B405-life-12-00495">405</a>,<a href="#B406-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B406-life-12-00495">406</a>,<a href="#B407-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B407-life-12-00495">407</a>,<a href="#B408-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B408-life-12-00495">408</a>] and earlier onset of cardiovascular disease in males [<a href="#B409-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B409-life-12-00495">409</a>], and postmenopausal osteoporosis [<a href="#B410-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B410-life-12-00495">410</a>,<a href="#B411-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B411-life-12-00495">411</a>] and IBS [<a href="#B412-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B412-life-12-00495">412</a>,<a href="#B413-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B413-life-12-00495">413</a>] in females. This relationship has also been implicated in ovarian cancer [<a href="#B414-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B414-life-12-00495">414</a>,<a href="#B415-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B415-life-12-00495">415</a>] and polycystic ovary syndrome [<a href="#B416-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B416-life-12-00495">416</a>,<a href="#B417-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B417-life-12-00495">417</a>]. Sex hormones have been shown to affect the immune system through interactions with the gut microbiome, including influencing the gut barrier permeability and interacting with immune cells [<a href="#B418-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B418-life-12-00495">418</a>,<a href="#B419-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B419-life-12-00495">419</a>]. Studies show that there are sex-specific differences in the immune system, which are independent of the gut microbiota and are already present in germ-free mice, suggesting that the immune system can select a gender-specific gut microbiome conformation, which also plays a part in the differential influence on the immune system observed across genders [<a href="#B420-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B420-life-12-00495">420</a>]. These gender-specific differences in immunity result in males being more susceptible to infections [<a href="#B421-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B421-life-12-00495">421</a>], and females being more susceptible to autoimmune disorders [<a href="#B422-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B422-life-12-00495">422</a>]. </p>
<p>The implications of microbial dysbiosis on astronaut health can be better evaluated when the gender-specific distinctions between male and female microbiomes are considered. Astronauts’ microbiomes are exposed to and influenced by many intense factors in space, and there is evidence that these factors have a gender bias. Astronauts are exposed to cosmic radiation, which has been observed to alter the gut microbiome composition [<a href="#B423-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B423-life-12-00495">423</a>]. Cui et al. conducted animal studies with mice to show that the effect of radiation toxicity is more prevalent on female gut microbiomes and that gender-matched fecal microbiota transplantation was most successful in reversing these effects [<a href="#B424-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B424-life-12-00495">424</a>]. Females are also more vulnerable to radiation-induced cancer than males, especially radiation-induced breast, lung, thyroid and ovarian cancer [<a href="#B425-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B425-life-12-00495">425</a>,<a href="#B426-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B426-life-12-00495">426</a>]. Looking more closely at the microbiomes of Chernobyl victims or nuclear power plant workers may be an area of interest to further investigate the long-term effects of radiation [<a href="#B427-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B427-life-12-00495">427</a>,<a href="#B428-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B428-life-12-00495">428</a>]. There are also extensive lifestyle changes during spaceflight, which include alterations to circadian rhythms and modifications to the diet. Changes in sleep patterns have been observed to have an influence on the gut microbiome, leading to a higher risk of breast cancer in females [<a href="#B429-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B429-life-12-00495">429</a>,<a href="#B430-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B430-life-12-00495">430</a>,<a href="#B431-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B431-life-12-00495">431</a>,<a href="#B432-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B432-life-12-00495">432</a>]. Diet has a sex-specific effect on the microbiome, where variations in changes to the microbiome composition have been observed in the presence of different diets and prebiotics [<a href="#B398-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B398-life-12-00495">398</a>,<a href="#B433-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B433-life-12-00495">433</a>,<a href="#B434-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B434-life-12-00495">434</a>,<a href="#B435-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B435-life-12-00495">435</a>]. Dietary fibers can affect estrogen levels [<a href="#B436-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B436-life-12-00495">436</a>], whose link with the microbiome has been recognized, and high-fat high-sugar diets can affect bile acid production differently across genders, which also has been shown to influence the gut microbiome [<a href="#B398-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B398-life-12-00495">398</a>]. </p>
<p>Astronauts experience high levels of stress in space due to a multitude of factors, including isolation, resulting in higher levels of cortisol and catecholamines [<a href="#B437-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B437-life-12-00495">437</a>]. Increased levels of stress-induced cortisol can trigger an inflammation response, disrupt the intestinal barrier and alter microbial composition [<a href="#B438-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B438-life-12-00495">438</a>,<a href="#B439-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B439-life-12-00495">439</a>,<a href="#B440-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B440-life-12-00495">440</a>]. As there are sex-specific differences between the way males and females regulate their stress response, the impact on the microbiome varies [<a href="#B441-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B441-life-12-00495">441</a>,<a href="#B442-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B442-life-12-00495">442</a>]. Studies examining the effect of isolation on prairie voles showed gender-specific changes in the gut microbiome composition [<a href="#B443-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B443-life-12-00495">443</a>]. Dietary supplementation of DHA reduced stress and changed the microbiome composition in socially isolated male mice, but not in female mice, further suggesting the impact of sex-specific stress responses on the microbiome [<a href="#B444-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B444-life-12-00495">444</a>]. Cortisol can also negatively impact the vaginal microbiome by inhibiting the glycogen deposition, which can lead to genitourinary tract infections [<a href="#B445-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B445-life-12-00495">445</a>]. Therefore, the associated stress of spaceflight has a different impact on male and female microbiomes, which may contribute to the gender-specific associated health risks of space travel. </p>
<p>The disproportion of men compared to women who have flown to space introduces a bias to the data collected from astronauts and the available evidence limits the conclusions that can be made on the impacts of space exploration on female health. Recognizing gender-specific differences in the microbiome response to the extreme factors of space will allow for better and more personalized countermeasures and medical care to help preserve the homeostasis of the microbiome and as a result, the health of astronauts. </p></section><section id="sec8-life-12-00495"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">8. Pharmogenetics, Spaceflight and the Microbiome </h2>
<p>Even with countermeasures and preventions put in place to keep astronauts safe during long-duration missions, the extended time in space with prolonged exposure to radiation, weightlessness, and other stressors on the body will inevitably lead to medical issues that arise during flight. These ailments will need to be addressed by pharmaceutical intervention with even more robustness and rigor than in low-earth orbit, due to the inability for emergency evacuation and limited non-medicinal interventions. An excellent review by Blue et al. discusses the challenges and current understanding for NASA in supplying a pharmacy for exploration spaceflight [<a href="#B446-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B446-life-12-00495">446</a>]. </p>
<p>Medication has been used for decades by astronauts during spaceflight to help relieve symptoms such as headaches and muscle/joint pain [<a href="#B447-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B447-life-12-00495">447</a>], or for more serious issues, such as treating jugular venous thrombosis [<a href="#B448-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B448-life-12-00495">448</a>]. Medical toolkits onboard the ISS now contain about 190 different frequently used pharmaceuticals. However, observational studies and anecdotal reports from crew members indicate medications to be “not effective” or “less effective” at managing their complaints (<a href="#life-12-00495-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>) [<a href="#B446-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B446-life-12-00495">446</a>,<a href="#B449-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B449-life-12-00495">449</a>,<a href="#B450-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B450-life-12-00495">450</a>]. More formal studies support these reports and suggest altered drug disposition in space compared to Earth, which include differences in efficacy, absorption, drug elimination, pro-drug activation and build-up of toxic metabolites [<a href="#B446-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B446-life-12-00495">446</a>,<a href="#B447-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B447-life-12-00495">447</a>]. </p>
<p>While human physiological changes [<a href="#B451-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B451-life-12-00495">451</a>,<a href="#B452-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B452-life-12-00495">452</a>] and reduced drug self-life [<a href="#B453-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B453-life-12-00495">453</a>,<a href="#B454-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B454-life-12-00495">454</a>] during spaceflight can alter drug disposition in space, the gut microbiome can also contribute to differential drug efficacy and safety, by enzymatically transforming drug structure and altering drug bioavailability, bioactivity, or toxicity [<a href="#B455-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B455-life-12-00495">455</a>,<a href="#B456-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B456-life-12-00495">456</a>]. For example, the common human gut bacterium, <em>Eggerthella lenta</em>, inactivates the cardiac drug digoxin, used to treat heart failure and arrythmia, via a two-gene ‘cardiac glycoside reductase’ (<em>cgr</em>) operon, which is conserved and widespread within the human-associated gut microbiome [<a href="#B457-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B457-life-12-00495">457</a>]. It has also been shown that co-culture of <em>E. lenta</em> with the fecal microbiome increased <em>cgr</em> expression, thereby enhancing digoxin metabolism and inactivation [<a href="#B458-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B458-life-12-00495">458</a>], reminding us of the interconnectedness of microbial species within a community and its impact on the host. Of interest, dietary protein was able to reduce digoxin metabolism [<a href="#B458-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B458-life-12-00495">458</a>], thereby maintaining its efficacy. This has important implications for the crew as a carefully considered “microbiome diet” could help ameliorate any negative effects that may be imposed by a dynamic and changing microbiome caused by spaceflight. </p>
<p>The gut microbiome can also promote drug activation, which was first discovered in 1937, with the antibiotic prontosil, which required bacterial azoreductases in the gut to cleave the drug into its active form [<a href="#B459-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B459-life-12-00495">459</a>]. Since then, other prodrugs have been developed, such as sulfasalazine, balsalazide, and olsalazine, used to treat ulcerative colitis, which rely on colonic bacteria for activation [<a href="#B460-life-12-00495" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B460-life-12-00495">460</a>].
